Distinction Between Mashi'a and Irada in Talaq
Explains why mashi'a effects divorce but irada does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mashi'a and irada in the context of delegated divorce and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Mashi'a triggers divorce because it implies existence; irada does not.

In Hanafi fiqh, the choice of words in divorce delegation matters greatly. The husband's utterance of 'I desire your divorce' does not cause a divorce when the wife assents, because irada is only an intention. But if he uses 'I wish', it does. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage contrasts two situations: (1) delegation with irada/mahabba/hawa where the wife's response has no effect, and (2) conditioning divorce on irada etc. where satisfaction of the condition does effect divorce.
Delegation vs explicit condition: different rulings.
2
Analyze linguistic derivation
Examine that mashi'a derives from شيء (thing/existent), so it implies actualization. Irada is from طلب (request), which does not necessitate existence.
Hanafi usul: linguistic roots determine legal effect.
3
Apply the distinction to novel cases
Given a scenario with other words like 'amr' (command) or 'tamanni' (wishing), decide whether they resemble mashi'a or irada based on linguistic implication of existence.
The illa is existential force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لها : أريدي طلاقك ينوي الطلاق فقالت: أردت، لا يقع، وكذا لو قال لها : أحبي طلاقك أو أهوي، ففعلت لم يقع شيء، والفرق أن المشيئة تنبئ عن الوجود؛ لأنها من الشيء وهو الموجود فكان شئت" بمنزلة أو جدت وأما الإرادة فهي الطلب لغة وليس من ضرورة الطلب الوجود. والمحبة والهواء نوع تمني بخلاف ما إذا قال لها : أنت طالق إن أردت أو أجبت أو رضيت أو هويت، ففعلت حيث يقع لوجود الشرط. (عيني، مستخلص)
And if he said to her: I intend/desire your divorce, intending divorce, and she said: I intended/desired, it does not occur. Similarly, if he said to her: Love your divorce or desire it, and she did so, nothing occurs. The difference is that mashi'a (wish) indicates existence because it is derived from 'thing' (شيء) which is existent, so 'I wish' (شئت) is like 'I caused to exist'. As for irada (intention/desire), it is request (طلب) linguistically, and existence is not necessary for request. Love (محبة) and desire (هوى) are types of wishful thinking. Unlike if he said to her: You are divorced if you intend/desire, or accept, or are pleased, or desire (هويت), and she did so, where it does occur, because the condition exists. (عيني، مستخلص)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling that clarifies the legal distinction between the terms 'mashi'a' (مشيئة) and 'irada' (إرادة) in the context of divorce delegation. When the husband says 'I intend/desire your divorce' (أريدي طلاقك) or 'love your divorce' (أحبي طلاقك) or 'desire it' (أهوي), and the wife responds accordingly, no divorce occurs. The reason is that these words (irada, mahabba, hawa) indicate a mental state of wanting or wishing, but they do not imply actualization. In contrast, 'mashi'a' (shā'a = to will) is derived from 'shay' (thing) which means an existing entity; thus 'sh'it' (I wished) is equivalent to 'awjadtu' (I caused to exist). Therefore, when the husband uses the word 'mashi'a' in delegation, the wife's statement 'sh'it' effects the divorce because it actualizes the conditional divorce. However, if the husband uses 'irada' or similar terms in the condition itself (e.g., 'you are divorced if you intend/desire'), then the fulfillment of that condition by the wife does cause divorce because the husband made it a condition. The key distinction: mashi'a implies existence by its linguistic root, while irada does not. This is a Hanafi-specific nuance.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashi'a): Willing that implies existence; derived from شيء (thing, existent). In delegated divorce, it actualizes the divorce.
إرادة (irada): Intention or desire; linguistically 'request' (طلب), does not imply existence in the external world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband says 'I desire your divorce' (أريدي طلاقك) intending divorce, and the wife says 'I desired' (أردت), what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs because the wife expressed desire
B) Divorce occurs because the husband intended it
C) No divorce occurs because irada does not imply existence
D) Divorce occurs only if the husband repeats the statement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes mashi'a from irada based on linguistic roots: only mashi'a implies existence and thus can effect divorce without an explicit condition.

Delegation of Divorce with Temporal Indefinite Phrases
Rulings on delegating divorce using temporal indefinite terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on delegation of divorce using temporal indefinite phrases (mata, idha) and explain why the wife may only effect one divorce.

Core Idea
Delegation with 'mata' and 'idha' grants the wife ownership of one divorce at any time, not multiple divorces, because the words generalize times, not actions.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce to his wife using phrases like 'You are divorced whenever you wish' (أنت طالق متى شئت), the wife is given the power to divorce herself. However, the extent of this power and its duration are governed by the linguistic nature of the words used. This lesson unpacks the rulings from the matn and its commentary, focusing on three key issues: the effect of rejection, the time limit, and the number of divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase and its legal effect
Recognize that phrases like 'mata shi't' and 'idha shi't' are valid forms of delegation (tafwid) of divorce. The wife is given the authority to divorce herself at her discretion.
2
Determine the effect of the wife's initial refusal
The text states 'لا يرتد' (it is not returned). If the wife says 'I do not wish', she does not lose the delegated authority; she may still exercise it later.
The delegation is not conditional on immediate acceptance; it remains in effect.
3
Determine if the wife must act in the same assembly
The ruling 'لا يتقيد بالمجلس' clarifies that the delegation is not restricted to the immediate assembly. She may divorce at any future time.
This differs from options like a sale option which may expire.
4
Determine how many divorces she may effect
The text explicitly says 'لا تطلق إلا واحدة' (she is not divorced except one). The wife can only give one divorce using this delegation, even if she repeats the phrase or at different times.
The reasoning is that the words generalize times (azman) but not actions (af'al).
5
Understand the reasoning (talil) behind the limitation
The limitation to one divorce is because words like 'mata' and 'idha' are general for all times, so the wife may divorce at any time she wishes. But they do not generalize actions, so she cannot effect a divorce after a divorce using the same delegation.
This prevents a triple divorce in one delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنت طالق متى شِئت أو متى ما شئتِ أو إذا شئتِ أو إذا ما شئتِ فردّت الأمر بأن قالت: لا أشاء وهي طلقة رجعية لا يرتد، ولا يتقيد بالمجلس، ولا تطلق إلا واحدة لا يرتد إلخ: [فيجوز لها أن تشاء بعده وتطلق نفسها في أي زمن شاءت؛ لأنه ليس بتمليك قبل المشيئة فلا يرتد. (ط، ع)] أما في كلمة "متى ومتى ما فلأنهما عامتان في الأوقات كلها فصار كأنه قال: "في أي وقت شئت"، وأما كلمة "إذا" و"إذا ما" فعندهما كمتى ومتى ما وعند أبي حنيفة وإن كان يستعمل للشرط كما يستعمل للوقت لكن الأمر صار بيدها فلم يخرج بالشك، فإذا كان كذلك لا يرتد الأمر بردها إن كان لها مشية الطلاق في الزمان الثاني، ولا يتقيد بالمجلس؛ لأنه ملكها الطلاق في الوقت الذي شاءت، فإن لم تشأ في هذا المجلس كانت لها المشيئة في مجلس آخر، وقوله: "لا تطلق إلا واحدة" أي ليس لها أن تطلق نفسها بذلك التفويض إلا واحدة؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لتعميم الأزمان، فتملك التطليق في كل زمان لا لتعميم الأفعال فلا تملك تطليقاً بعد تطليق. (مستخلص) بالمجلس: لأن هذه الألفاظ تعم الأوقات كلها فلها أن توقع في أي وقت شاءت كما لو نص عليه. (ع) ولا تطلق أي لا تملك المرأة أن تطلق نفسها بهذه الألفاظ إن شاءت. (ط، ع) إلا واحدة: أي طلقة واحدة؛ لأنها تعم الأزمان دون الأفعال. (ع)
You are divorced whenever you wish or whenever you wish or if you wish or if you wish, so she returns the matter by saying: I do not wish, and it is a revocable divorce. It is not returned, not restricted to the assembly, and she is not divorced except one [divorce]. 'It is not returned': [So she may wish after it and divorce herself at any time she wants, because it is not a tamlik before the wish, so it does not return. (ط، ع)] As for the word 'mata' and 'mata ma', they are general for all times, so it becomes as if he said: 'at any time you wish'. As for the word 'idha' and 'idha ma', according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) like mata and mata ma, and according to Abu Hanifa, although it is used for condition as well as time, the matter has come into her hand, so it does not leave due to doubt. So when that is the case, the matter is not returned by her rejection if she has the power of divorce in the second time, and it is not restricted to the assembly because he granted her ownership of divorce at the time she wishes. If she does not wish in this assembly, she has the wish in another assembly. And his statement: 'She is not divorced except one' means she may not divorce herself with that delegation except one, because these words generalize times, so she owns divorce in every time, not for generalizing actions, so she may not own a divorce after a divorce. (مستخلص) Restricted to the assembly: Because these words generalize all times, so she may effect it at any time she wants as if he explicitly stated it. (ع) And she does not divorce: Meaning she does not own divorcing herself with these words if she wishes. (ط، ع) Except one: meaning one divorce, because they generalize times not actions. (ع)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on the effect of using temporal indefinite particles like 'mata' (whenever), 'idha' (if/when), etc., in delegating divorce to the wife. The main rulings: (1) The delegation is not returned (لا يرتد) to the husband; the wife retains the power even if she initially says 'I do not wish'. (2) It is not restricted to the immediate assembly (لا يتقيد بالمجلس); the wife can act on it at any later time. (3) Only one divorce can be effected (لا تطلق إلا واحدة); the wife cannot use the same delegation to give multiple divorces because the words generalize times, not actions. The commentary by al-'Ayni and others explains that 'mata' and 'mata ma' are general for all times, so it is as if the husband said 'at any time you wish'. As for 'idha' and 'idha ma', the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat them like 'mata' in this context, while Abu Hanifa's position is that although 'idha' can be used for condition as well as time, since the delegation has been given to the wife, it remains operative and does not revert by doubt. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) but the relied-upon position is not specified here. The key benefit: the wife does not lose the power by not using it immediately, and she cannot use it to give three divorces at once.

Key Terms
متى (mata): When/Whenever; used as a condition for delegation, generalized over time
إذا (idha): If/When; Abu Hanifa treats it like mata in delegation contexts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' (أنت طالق متى شئت), how many divorces may the wife effect using this delegation?
A) Unlimited
B) Three
C) One
D) None, because delegation is invalid without specifying time

Key Takeaway
A general temporal delegation of divorce grants the wife the power to effect one revocable divorce at any time, and her initial refusal does not revoke the delegation.

Delegation with 'Kullama' – One by One, Limited to Existing Ownership
Ruling on delegating divorce using 'kullama' with intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delegation using 'kullama', including the limitation after remarriage, and explain the disagreement with Zufar.

Core Idea
Kullama allows one divorce at a time, but only within the current marriage; after remarriage it’s void.

When a husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' (kullama shi't), the wife gains the ability to divorce herself, but the delegation is tied to the existing marriage. This lesson unpacks how the word 'kullama' operates, why divorces must be separated, and what happens if the wife remarries after three divorces.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of kullama
Recognize that 'kullama' generalizes over times and actions individually, not collectively. This means each occurrence allows a single divorce.
Arabic grammar: kullama implies repetition of individual actions.
2
Determine the effect on divorce
The wife may give herself one divorce at a time until three are given. She cannot combine two or three in one pronouncement.
This is the school's position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
3
Identify the limitation after remarriage
If the wife gives three separate divorces and later remarries the same husband, the delegation does not carry over. The new marriage is a separate ownership.
The oath is tied to the original ownership (al-milk al-qa'im).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar disagrees: he holds that ownership is not a condition for the oath to remain, so the delegation would still be effective. The majority rejects this.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلّما شئت لها أن تفرق الثلاث ولا تجمع، ولو طلقت بعد زوج آخر لا يقع، وفي حيث شئتِ وأين شئتِ لم تطلق وفي :كلما أي في قوله لها: أنت طالق كلما إلخ. (ع) أن تفرق الثلاث إلخ [بأن تطلق نفسها واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً ولا توقع الثلاث جملة واحدة. (ط،ع)] أي لو قال لها: أنت طالق كلما شئت فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة بعد واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً؛ لأن كلمة "كلما" تعم الأوقات والأفعال عموم الأفراد لا عموم الاجتماع أي توجب عموم الفعل فردا لا جملة فيقتضي إيقاع الواحدة في كل مرة إلى ما لا يتناهى إلا أن اليمين ينصرف رف إلى الملك القائم؛ لأن صحتها باعتباره فلا تملك الإيقاع بعد وقوع الثلاث إذا رجعت إليه بعد زوج آخر مع صلاحية اللفظ له، وهو المراد بقوله: "ولو طلقت بعد زوج آخر لا يقع"، وقال زفر: يقع؛ لأن الملك عنده ليس بشرط لبقاء اليمين، ولهذا لو قال لها: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، طلقها ثلاثاً قبل أن تدخل ثم عادت إليه بعد زوج آخر ثم دخلت الدار طلقت ثلاثاً عنده، وعندنا: لا يقع؛ لأن الملك بعد زوج آخر لملك مستحدث وليس بالملك القائم. (عيني، مستخلص) ولا تجمع : بأن تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً أو ثنتين في كلمة واحدة (ط) زوج آخر وبعد رجوعها إليه بعد الطلقات الثلاث. [رمز الحقائق: ١٥٢/١] لا يقع: إن كانت طلقت نفسها ثلاثاً متفرقة وإلا فلها تفريقها بعد زوج آخر. (ط) وفي حيث: أي في قول الزوج: أنت طالق حيث إلخ. (ط) لم تطلق إلخ فإن قامت من مجلسها فلا مشية لها؛ لأن "حيث" و"أين" للمكان، ولا يعلق الطلاق به ،فيلغو ويبقى ذكر مطلق المشيئة فيقتصر على المشيئة، بخلاف الزمان؛ لأن له تعلقاً به، حتى يقع في زمان دون زمان، فوجب اعتباره خصوصاً، كقوله: "أنت طالق غداً" أو نحوه، وعموماً كقوله: "أنت طالق في أي وقت شئت" ونحوه، فإن قلت: إذا لغا ذكر المكان يبقى قوله: = ثم
And in (the phrase) 'whenever you wish', she may separate the three and not combine. And if she divorces after another husband, it does not occur. And in (the phrase) 'wherever you wish' and 'wherever you wish', she is not divorced. 'And in (the phrase) whenever you wish': i.e., in his saying to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish' etc. (ع) 'She may separate the three' etc. [That she divorces herself one by one until she divorces three times and does not effect the three all at once. (ط،ع)] That is if he said to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish', she may divorce herself one after another until she divorces three times, because the word 'kullama' generalizes times and actions in an individual sense, not in a collective sense, meaning it necessitates generality of action as individuals, not as a whole, so it necessitates effecting one at a time indefinitely, except that the oath is directed to the existing ownership, because its validity is based on it. So she does not have the right to effect after three divorces if she returns to him after another husband, even if the wording could apply, and that is intended by his statement: 'And if she divorces after another husband, it does not occur'. And Zufar said: it occurs, because according to him ownership is not a condition for the oath to remain. For this reason, if he said to her: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times', and he divorced her three times before she entered, then she returned to him after another husband, then entered the house, she would be divorced three times according to him. According to us, it does not occur, because ownership after another husband is new ownership, not the existing ownership. (عيني، مستخلص) 'And not combine': that she divorces herself three or two in one word (ط) 'after another husband and after she returns to him after three divorces'. [رمز الحقائق: 152/1] 'It does not occur': if she divorced herself three separately, otherwise she may separate them after another husband. (ط) 'And in (the phrase) where': i.e., in the husband's saying: 'You are divorced where' etc. (ط) 'She is not divorced' etc.: For if she leaves her assembly, she has no wish, because 'haythu' and 'ayna' are for place, and divorce is not suspended on place, so it is disregarded and the mention of absolute wish remains, so it is confined to the wish. Unlike time, because it has relevance to it, such that it occurs in one time but not another, so it must be considered specifically, like his saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' or similar, and generally like his saying 'You are divorced at any time you wish' and similar. If you say: when the mention of place is disregarded, his statement remains = then
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement covering two distinct types of delegation phrases. First, 'kullama sh'it' (كلما شئت, 'whenever you wish'). The key rulings: (1) The wife may separate the three divorces, i.e., give herself one divorce at a time until three are given, but she may not combine them into a single pronouncement. (2) If she gives three divorces (separately) and then remarries after another husband, the delegation does not carry over; any further attempt to divorce is ineffective. This is because the oath is tied to the existing ownership (الملك القائم), and after a second marriage, the ownership is new. There is an opposition from Zufar who holds that ownership is not a condition for the oath to remain, but the Hanafi position (عندنا) is that it does not occur. The commentary also clarifies that the phrase 'لشيء مضى' earlier refers to past conditions. Second, for phrases using 'haythu' (wherever) or 'ayna' (where), the wife is not divorced because these words are for place, and divorce is not attached to place. They are disregarded, and only the absolute wish remains; but since the delegation was conditional on a place, it is void, and the wife's wish without the place condition does not effect divorce. This is contrasted with temporal phrases which have relevance to divorce. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Hanifa vs. Zufar) on the effect of after-another-husband.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): Whenever; used in delegation, generalizes over times and actions individually, allowing multiple divorces one by one
الملك القائم (al-milk al-qa'im): Existing ownership; the current marriage that is in effect when the oath is made

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a wife, after giving herself three separate divorces using a kullama delegation, remarries her husband and then attempts to give another divorce using the same wording?
A) She can, because the wording 'whenever' is general.
B) She cannot, because the delegation was tied to the original marriage and the new marriage is a separate ownership.
C) She can, but only if she had not intended to exhaust the delegation.
D) She cannot, because delegation is automatically revoked after any divorce.

Key Takeaway
Kullama delegation permits divorces one by one but only within the current marriage; after remarriage it has no effect.

Delegation with 'Haythu' and 'Ayna' – Place Does Not Affect Divorce
Ruling on delegating divorce using place-based phrases – they are disregarded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why place-based delegation phrases like 'haythu' and 'ayna' are legally ineffective in divorce and contrast them with time-based phrases.

Core Idea
Place words like 'wherever' are irrelevant to divorce; only time matters for delegation.

When a husband says 'You are divorced wherever you wish' (haythu shi't or ayna shi't), the inclusion of place is meaningless. This lesson explains why place has no bearing on divorce and how the phrase is treated, in contrast to time-based conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Recognize that the husband used 'haythu' (where) or 'ayna' (where) as a condition for the wife's wish.
These are place words.
2
Understand the rule: place is irrelevant
Divorce is not suspended on place; a change of location has no effect on marital status.
The text says 'divorce is not attached to place'.
3
Apply the rule: the phrase is disregarded
Since the condition (place) is irrelevant, it is struck out. Only the mention of 'wish' remains, but without a valid condition, the delegation is void.
The wife's wish alone, without a valid condition, does not effect divorce.
4
Contrast with time
Time has relevance; conditions like 'tomorrow' or 'at any time' are valid because divorce can occur at different times.
Examples: 'You are divorced tomorrow' or 'at any time you wish' are effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلّما شئت لها أن تفرق الثلاث ولا تجمع، ولو طلقت بعد زوج آخر لا يقع، وفي حيث شئتِ وأين شئتِ لم تطلق وفي :كلما أي في قوله لها: أنت طالق كلما إلخ. (ع) أن تفرق الثلاث إلخ [بأن تطلق نفسها واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً ولا توقع الثلاث جملة واحدة. (ط،ع)] أي لو قال لها: أنت طالق كلما شئت فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة بعد واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً؛ لأن كلمة "كلما" تعم الأوقات والأفعال عموم الأفراد لا عموم الاجتماع أي توجب عموم الفعل فردا لا جملة فيقتضي إيقاع الواحدة في كل مرة إلى ما لا يتناهى إلا أن اليمين ينصرف رف إلى الملك القائم؛ لأن صحتها باعتباره فلا تملك الإيقاع بعد وقوع الثلاث إذا رجعت إليه بعد زوج آخر مع صلاحية اللفظ له، وهو المراد بقوله: "ولو طلقت بعد زوج آخر لا يقع"، وقال زفر: يقع؛ لأن الملك عنده ليس بشرط لبقاء اليمين، ولهذا لو قال لها: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، طلقها ثلاثاً قبل أن تدخل ثم عادت إليه بعد زوج آخر ثم دخلت الدار طلقت ثلاثاً عنده، وعندنا: لا يقع؛ لأن الملك بعد زوج آخر لملك مستحدث وليس بالملك القائم. (عيني، مستخلص) ولا تجمع : بأن تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً أو ثنتين في كلمة واحدة (ط) زوج آخر وبعد رجوعها إليه بعد الطلقات الثلاث. (ط) لم تطلق إلخ فإن قامت من مجلسها فلا مشية لها؛ لأن "حيث" و"أين" للمكان، ولا يعلق الطلاق به ،فيلغو ويبقى ذكر مطلق المشيئة فيقتصر على المشيئة، بخلاف الزمان؛ لأن له تعلقاً به، حتى يقع في زمان دون زمان، فوجب اعتباره خصوصاً، كقوله: "أنت طالق غداً" أو نحوه، وعموماً كقوله: "أنت طالق في أي وقت شئت" ونحوه، فإن قلت: إذا لغا ذكر المكان يبقى قوله: = ثم

'And in (the phrase) whenever you wish': i.e., in his saying to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish' etc. (ع) 'She may separate the three' etc. [That she divorces herself one by one until she divorces three times and does not effect the three all at once. (ط،ع)] That is if he said to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish', she may divorce herself one after another until she divorces three times, because the word 'kullama' generalizes times and actions in an individual sense, not in a collective sense, meaning it necessitates generality of action as individuals, not as a whole, so it necessitates effecting one at a time indefinitely, except that the oath is directed to the existing ownership, because its validity is based on it. So she does not have the right to effect after three divorces if she returns to him after another husband, even if the wording could apply, and that is intended by his statement: 'And if she divorces after another husband, it does not occur'. And Zufar said: it occurs, because according to him ownership is not a condition for the oath to remain. For this reason, if he said to her: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times', and he divorced her three times before she entered, then she returned to him after another husband, then entered the house, she would be divorced three times according to him. According to us, it does not occur, because ownership after another husband is new ownership, not the existing ownership. (عيني، مستخلص) 'And not combine': that she divorces herself three or two in one word (ط) 'after another husband and after she returns to him after three divorces'. [رمز الحقائق: 152/1] 'It does not occur': if she divorced herself three separately, otherwise she may separate them after another husband. (ط) 'And in (the phrase) where': i.e., in the husband's saying: 'You are divorced where' etc. (ط) 'She is not divorced' etc.: For if she leaves her assembly, she has no wish, because 'haythu' and 'ayna' are for place, and divorce is not suspended on place, so it is disregarded and the mention of absolute wish remains, so it is confined to the wish. Unlike time, because it has relevance to it, such that it occurs in one time but not another, so it must be considered specifically, like his saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' or similar, and generally like his saying 'You are divorced at any time you wish' and similar. If you say: when the mention of place is disregarded, his statement remains = then
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement covering two distinct types of delegation phrases. First, 'kullama sh'it' (كلما شئت, 'whenever you wish'). The key rulings: (1) The wife may separate the three divorces, i.e., give herself one divorce at a time until three are given, but she may not combine them into a single pronouncement. (2) If she gives three divorces (separately) and then remarries after another husband, the delegation does not carry over; any further attempt to divorce is ineffective. This is because the oath is tied to the existing ownership (الملك القائم), and after a second marriage, the ownership is new. There is an opposition from Zufar who holds that ownership is not a condition for the oath to remain, but the Hanafi position (عندنا) is that it does not occur. The commentary also clarifies that the phrase 'لشيء مضى' earlier refers to past conditions. Second, for phrases using 'haythu' (wherever) or 'ayna' (where), the wife is not divorced because these words are for place, and divorce is not attached to place. They are disregarded, and only the absolute wish remains; but since the delegation was conditional on a place, it is void, and the wife's wish without the place condition does not effect divorce. This is contrasted with temporal phrases which have relevance to divorce. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Hanifa vs. Zufar) on the effect of after-another-husband.

Key Terms
حيث (haythu): Where; used for place, disregarded in divorce delegation because place is irrelevant
أين (ayna): Where; similar to haythu, also disregarded

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is divorced because she fulfilled the condition.
B) She is not divorced because the place condition is irrelevant and the delegation is void.
C) She is divorced only if she stands up.
D) She is not divorced because the husband must specify a time.

Key Takeaway
Place-based delegation phrases like 'wherever' are legally ineffective; only time-based conditions matter in divorce.

General Principles of the Delegated Divorce Option
Explanation of general rules of the option and specific words for time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three principles of the delegation of divorce option and distinguish the linguistic treatment of 'mata' and 'idha' in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Once the husband delegates the divorce option, he cannot revoke it; the wife may choose at any time but only once.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies key rules when a husband delegates the right to divorce to his wife using conditional phrases like 'mata shi'ti' (whenever you wish) or 'idha shi'ti' (if you wish). The commentary addresses three core principles: the delegation is irrevocable, the wife's option is not confined to the immediate session, and she can only effect a single divorce through that delegation. Additionally, the passage examines the linguistic scope of the words 'mata' and 'idha', noting a difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the latter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Core Ruling
The act under discussion is the husband's delegation (tamlik) of the divorce right to his wife. The core ruling is that once the delegation is made, it is not revocable (la yartadd). The wife may exercise her option at any time.
This distinguishes it from a mere offer that can be withdrawn.
2
Determine the Temporal Scope of the Option
The wife's option is not restricted to the session in which the delegation was made (la yataqayyad bi al-majlis). She may divorce in any subsequent session because the words 'mata' etc. generalize all times.
The Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa agree on this point despite lexical nuances.
3
Count the Number of Divorces Permitted
The wife can only effect one divorce through this delegation (la tuttallaq illa wahidah). The words generalize times, not actions, so she cannot divorce multiple times.
If she divorces once, the delegation is exhausted.
4
Analyze the Linguistic Treatment of 'Mata' and 'Idha'
For 'mata' and 'mata ma', both are general for all times. For 'idha' and 'idha ma', the Sahibayn hold it is like 'mata' (general), while Abu Hanifa holds that even though 'idha' has a conditional usage, the context of delegation overrides any doubt, making it also general.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the practical ruling that the option is unrestricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يرتد إلخ: [فيجوز لها أن تشاء بعده وتطلق نفسها في أي زمن شاءت؛ لأنه ليس بتمليك قبل المشيئة فلا يرتد. (ط، ع)] أما في كلمة "متى ومتى ما فلأنهما عامتان في الأوقات كلها فصار كأنه قال: "في أي وقت شئت"، وأما كلمة "إذا" و"إذا ما" فعندهما كمتى ومتى ما وعند أبي حنيفة وإن كان يستعمل للشرط كما يستعمل للوقت لكن الأمر صار بيدها فلم يخرج بالشك، فإذا كان كذلك لا يرتد الأمر بردها إن كان لها مشية الطلاق في الزمان الثاني، ولا يتقيد بالمجلس؛ لأنه ملكها الطلاق في الوقت الذي شاءت، فإن لم تشأ في هذا المجلس كانت لها المشيئة في مجلس آخر، وقوله: "لا تطلق إلا واحدة" أي ليس لها أن تطلق نفسها بذلك التفويض إلا واحدة؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ لتعميم الأزمان، فتملك التطليق في كل زمان لا لتعميم الأفعال فلا تملك تطليقاً بعد تطليق. (مستخلص) بالمجلس:
He said: 'It is not revoked, etc.' [She may will after it and divorce herself in any time she wills, because it is not a tamlik before the will, so it is not revoked. (T, A)] As for the word 'mata' and 'mata ma', they are general for all times, so it becomes as if he said: 'In any time you wish'. As for the word 'idha' and 'idha ma', according to them (Sahibayn) it is like 'mata' and 'mata ma', but according to Abu Hanifa, although it is used for condition as it is used for time, the matter has been put in her hand, so it is not taken out by doubt. Thus, the matter is not revoked by her rejection if she has the option of divorce in the second time. It is not restricted to the session because he made her owner of divorce at whatever time she wills. If she does not will in this session, she has the option in another session. His statement: 'She does not divorce except one' means she may not divorce herself with that delegation except one, because these words are for generalizing times, so she owns divorce in every time, not for generalizing actions, so she does not own divorce after divorce. (Mukhtalas) by the session.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is an exposition (bayan) of the ruling of the option (mashi'ah) in divorce. The commentator explains three key principles drawn from the matn. First, 'la yartad' (it is not revoked): once the husband delegates the option to the wife by saying 'When you wish, you are divorced', this delegation is not revocable by him before she exercises her choice. This is because it is not a tamlik (transfer of ownership) before the will; it is a conditional authorization. Second, 'la yataqayyad bi al-majlis' (it is not restricted to the session): the wife may exercise her option at any time, not necessarily in the same sitting, because the words 'mata' etc. generalize all times. Third, 'la tuttallaq illa wahidah' (she divorces only one): she cannot effect more than one divorce through this delegation, because the words generalize times, not actions—she owns one divorce in any time, not multiple divorces. The commentary then elaborates on the words 'mata' and 'idha'. For 'mata' and 'mata ma', both are general for all times, so the wife may divorce at any time. For 'idha' and 'idha ma', there is a difference: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), they function like 'mata' and are general, but according to Abu Hanifa, 'idha' is used for condition as well as time, yet because the husband put the matter in her hand, it is not affected by doubt, so it also generalizes all times. The passage also clarifies that if she rejects the option (by saying 'I do not will'), the delegation is not revoked; she still has the option later because it was not a tamlik before the will. The overall ruling is that this delegation gives her the power to divorce once at any time she chooses.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The wife's choice to divorce herself when the husband has delegated the right using words like 'whenever you wish'.
يرتد (yartadd): The husband's revocation of the delegation before the wife exercises her option.
يتقيد بالمجلس (yataqayyad bi al-majlis): The requirement that the wife must exercise her option in the same sitting in which the delegation was made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens if the husband says 'Idha shi'ti fanti taliq' and the wife does not will in the same session?
A) She loses the option and cannot divorce later.
B) She may will in another session because the option is not restricted to the session.
C) The husband may revoke the delegation.
D) She is automatically divorced.

Key Takeaway
A husband's delegation of the divorce option is binding, not revocable, and gives the wife the power to divorce once at any time, regardless of whether she rejects initially.

Ruling on 'Kullama Shi't' and Dispute with Zufar
Explanation of 'kullama shi't' and dispute with Zufar on effect after second marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on 'kullama shi't' and evaluate Zufar's contrary position regarding the effect after a new marriage.

Core Idea
A delegation of divorce tied to the existing marriage does not revive after a second marriage because the property is new.

This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Zufar regarding the scope of a delegated divorce when the wife remarries the same husband after three divorces. The key lies in whether the conditional oath (yamin) is tied to the original marriage property or remains independent. Understanding this distinction clarifies a critical boundary in the law of ta'liq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation formula
The husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' using the word 'kullama'. Recognize that this delegates the power to divorce to the wife.
The phrase 'kullama shi't' is a common delegation form.
2
Understand the Hanafi interpretation of 'kullama'
Kullama generalizes times and actions as individuals, meaning the wife may divorce one at a time, not combine multiple divorces. She can repeat until three.
This is based on the linguistic analysis of 'kullama' as 'عموم الأفراد'.
3
Note the limitation: dependence on existing marriage property
The oath (yamin) is directed towards the existing marriage property (milk qa'im). Its validity depends on that property. After three divorces and a subsequent marriage, the property is new (milk mustahdath), so the oath does not apply.
This is the core Hanafi argument.
4
Compare with Zufar's position
Zufar argues property is not a condition for the oath's continuance. He analogizes to a conditional divorce ('If you enter the house, you are divorced thrice') which would still occur after remarriage.
Zufar uses qiyas to support his view.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Hanafi, the wife cannot use the delegation after remarriage. Under Zufar, she could. The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the divorce occurs.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي :كلما أي في قوله لها: أنت طالق كلما إلخ. (ع) أن تفرق الثلاث إلخ [بأن تطلق نفسها واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً ولا توقع الثلاث جملة واحدة. (ط،ع)] أي لو قال لها: أنت طالق كلما شئت فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة بعد واحدة حتى تطلق ثلاثاً؛ لأن كلمة "كلما" تعم الأوقات والأفعال عموم الأفراد لا عموم الاجتماع أي توجب عموم الفعل فردا لا جملة فيقتضي إيقاع الواحدة في كل مرة إلى ما لا يتناهى إلا أن اليمين ينصرف رف إلى الملك القائم؛ لأن صحتها باعتباره فلا تملك الإيقاع بعد وقوع الثلاث إذا رجعت إليه بعد زوج آخر مع صلاحية اللفظ له، وهو المراد بقوله: "ولو طلقت بعد زوج آخر لا يقع"، وقال زفر: يقع؛ لأن الملك عنده ليس بشرط لبقاء اليمين، ولهذا لو قال لها: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، طلقها ثلاثاً قبل أن تدخل ثم عادت إليه بعد زوج آخر ثم دخلت الدار طلقت ثلاثاً عنده، وعندنا: لا يقع؛ لأن الملك بعد زوج آخر لملك مستحدث وليس بالملك القائم. (عيني، مستخلص) ولا تجمع : بأن تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً أو ثنتين في كلمة واحدة (ط) زوج آخر وبعد رجوعها إليه بعد الطلقات الثلاث. [رمز الحقائق: ١٥٢/١] لا يقع: إن كانت طلقت نفسها ثلاثاً متفرقة وإلا فلها تفريقها بعد زوج آخر. (ط)
And regarding 'kullama': i.e., in his statement to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish, etc.' (A) that she separate the three, etc. [i.e., by divorcing herself one by one until she divorces three, and she does not effect the three all at once. (T, A)] i.e., if he said to her: 'You are divorced whenever you wish', she may divorce herself one after another until she divorces three times, because the word 'kullama' generalizes times and actions in the sense of generality of individuals, not generality of combination: it requires the generality of the action as individuals, not as a whole, thus it necessitates the occurrence of one each time ad infinitum, except that the oath (yamin) is directed towards the existing property (milk qa'im), because its validity depends on it. Thus she does not own the occurrence after the three have occurred if she returns to him after another husband, even though the wording would allow it. This is what is meant by his statement: 'And if she divorced after another husband, it does not occur.' Zufar said: It occurs, because in his view property is not a condition for the continuance of the oath. For this reason, if he said to her: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced thrice', and he divorced her three times before she entered, then she returned to him after another husband, then entered the house, she would be divorced three times according to him. According to us, it does not occur because the property after another husband is a new property (milk mustahdath) and not the existing property. (Ayni, Mukhtalas) And do not combine: i.e., by divorcing herself three or two in one word. (T) After another husband and after her return to him after the three divorces. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/152] It does not occur: if she divorced herself three times separately, otherwise she may separate them after another husband. (T)
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when the husband uses the phrase 'kullama shi't' (whenever you wish). The key point is that the word 'kullama' generalizes both times and actions in the sense of individuals: she may divorce herself one at a time, repeatedly, until she completes three divorces. She cannot combine multiple divorces in one instance. However, there is a limit: once she has divorced three times, if she later marries another husband and then returns to the first husband, the delegation does not revive, because the validity of a conditional oath (yamin) depends on the existence of the marriage property (milk qa'im) that existed at the time of the oath. After a second marriage, it is a new property, so the oath does not apply. Zufar disagreed: he held that the oath continues even after a new marriage, because in his view property is not a condition for the oath's continuance. He analogized it to a conditional divorce: if a man says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced thrice' and then divorces her before entry, then she remarries him and enters, she is divorced thrice according to Zufar. But the Hanafi school rejects this, holding that the new marriage is a new property not covered by the original oath. The text also clarifies that the prohibition on combining applies to effecting three or two divorces in one word; she must separate them.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A word meaning 'whenever', used by the husband to delegate the option to the wife. It generalizes times and actions in the sense of individuals.
الملك القائم (al-milk al-qa'im): The existing marriage property that exists at the time of the oath/delegation.
اليمين (al-yamin): The conditional oath or vow that suspends divorce on a condition; here it refers to the delegated option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' and the wife divorces herself three times separately, then after a second marriage to the same husband, can she divorce again using the same delegation according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the delegation is permanent.
B) No, because the delegation is tied to the original marriage property which no longer exists.
C) Yes, according to Zufar, but not according to the majority.
D) It depends on whether the second marriage is after three divorces.

Key Takeaway
The delegation of divorce using 'kullama' is limited to the existing marriage; once the marriage ends, the delegation does not revive after a subsequent marriage.

Ruling for 'Haythu Shi't' and 'Ayna Shi't' – No Divorce, Option Limited to Session
Explanation of 'haythu' and 'ayna' – location words invalidate the condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband uses place words in talaq delegation and explain why the option is limited to the session.

Core Idea
Place words in talaq are invalid; the option becomes a simple will restricted to the session.

When a husband says 'You are divorced where you wish' (haythu shi'ti) or 'wherever you wish' (ayna shi'ti), the place word is legally ineffective because divorce does not depend on location. The remaining simple option must be exercised in the same session, otherwise it lapses. In this lesson, we will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the subtle distinction between place and time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband says 'ant taliq haythu shi'ti' – what is the ruling? She is not divorced unless she wills, and the will must be in the session.
The ruling is that the place word is null; the option is a simple delegation.
2
Locate the dalil
The reasoning is rational (dalil aqli): divorce cannot be suspended on place because place has no effect. The author analogizes place words to conditional words.
The proof is based on similarity between zarf and shart.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The similarity between zarf (adverb) and shart (condition) allows interpreting the place word as a conditional metaphor, preserving the delay.
When literal meaning is impossible, it becomes a metaphor – isti'ara.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage does not mention disputes, but it contrasts time (which is valid) with place.
Time is valid because divorce is connected to time; place is not.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position is that the option is confined to the session; if she leaves, it is void.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah and followed by later Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي حيث: أي في قول الزوج: أنت طالق حيث إلخ. (ط) لم تطلق إلخ فإن قامت من مجلسها فلا مشية لها؛ لأن "حيث" و"أين" للمكان، ولا يعلق الطلاق به، فيلغو ويبقى ذكر مطلق المشيئة فيقتصر على المشيئة، بخلاف الزمان؛ لأن له تعلقاً به، حتى يقع في زمان دون زمان، فوجب اعتباره خصوصاً، كقوله: "أنت طالق غداً" أو نحوه، وعموماً كقوله: "أنت طالق في أي وقت شئت" ونحوه، فإن قلت: إذا لغا ذكر المكان يبقى قوله: "أنت طالق شئت"، فينبغي أن يقع في الحال فلم يتعلق، قلنا: يحمل الظرف على الشرط لمناسبة بينهما من حيث أن الظرف يجامع المظروف كما أن الشرط يجامع المشروط، فعند تعذر الظرف حقيقة يصير الشرط مجازاً؛ لأن كلا منهما يفيد ضربا من التأخير وهو أولى من إلغائه أصلاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٢/١](فتح) في مجلسها حتى لو قامت عنه وشاءت في مجلس آخر لا يقع شيء. (ط)
And regarding 'haythu': i.e., in the husband's statement: 'You are divorced where, etc.' (T) She is not divorced, etc. For if she leaves her session, she has no option, because 'haythu' and 'ayna' are for place, and divorce is not suspended on place, so it becomes null (yulgha) and the mention of the mere option remains, so it is confined to the option. This is unlike time because divorce has a connection to time, such that it occurs in some times and not others, so it must be considered particularly, as in his statement: 'You are divorced tomorrow' or similar, and generally as in his statement: 'You are divorced at any time you wish' and similar. If you say: When the mention of place becomes null, his statement remains: 'You are divorced, you wish', so it should occur immediately without being suspended. We reply: The adverb (zarf) is interpreted as a condition (shart) due to the similarity between them, in that the adverb encompasses the object (mazruf) just as the condition encompasses the conditioned (mashrut). So when the literal meaning of the adverb is impossible, it becomes a conditional metaphor, because each of them conveys a kind of delay, and this is better than completely ignoring it. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/252] (Fath) In her session: so much so that if she leaves it and wills in another session, nothing occurs. (T)
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when the husband uses words of place: 'haythu shi'ti' (where you wish) or 'ayna shi'ti' (wherever you wish). The commentator explains that such words are invalid for suspending divorce because divorce is not linked to place (it does not occur differently in different places). Therefore, the word of place is ignored (yulgha), and what remains is merely the mention of the option (mashi'ah). This remaining option is then treated like a simple delegation of the option without any condition, which, by default, is restricted to the session (majlis). So if the wife leaves the session without exercising the option, she loses it. The commentator also addresses a potential objection: if the place word is ignored, only 'ant taliq shi'ti' (you are divorced, you wish) remains, which should cause an immediate divorce without suspension. The answer is that the adverb of place is interpreted as a condition (shart) by way of similarity: just as an adverb encompasses its object, a condition encompasses its conditioned. When literal interpretation is impossible, it becomes a conditional metaphor, preserving the intended delay. This is better than complete nullification. The practical result is that she may only will in the same session.

Key Terms
حيث (haythu): A word meaning 'where', used by the husband but invalid for divorce because place has no bearing.
الظرف (al-zarf): The adverb of place (or time) in the phrase; here it is the word 'haythu' or 'ayna'.
المجلس (al-majlis): The sitting/session in which the husband made the statement; the option must be exercised in this same session.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; in the qiyas, the place word is interpreted as a condition to preserve the delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the place word (haythu/ayna) in a talaq delegation considered invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because place words are ambiguous.
B) Because divorce cannot be suspended on place; place has no effect on the occurrence of divorce.
C) Because the wife must always be asked for her opinion.
D) Because the session is the only valid condition.

Key Takeaway
Place words in talaq delegation are invalid; the option becomes a simple will that must be exercised in the same session, otherwise it lapses.

The 'Kayfa Shi'ti' Dispute: Immediate Divorce vs. Delegated Choice
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'kayfa shi'ti' with linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the phrase 'kayfa shi'ti' and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's position, and apply the ruling in the two cases of practical difference.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'as you wish' in divorce, Abu Hanifa holds the divorce occurs immediately; the Sahibayn suspend it on her will.

This passage delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'kayfa shi'ti' ('as you wish') in the context of divorce. The core disagreement is whether such a statement effects an immediate revocable divorce, as Abu Hanifa holds, or whether it suspends the entire divorce on the wife's later choice, as the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq) and for distinguishing between immediate and conditional divorce in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the phrase
Recognize that the husband's statement 'anti taliq kayfa shi'ti' is the subject. The dispute is whether the divorce occurs immediately or is suspended on her will.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that one revocable divorce falls immediately. His proof is linguistic: 'kayfa' is interrogative, and the description (kayfiyya) requires the existence of the described (divorce).
The Arabic example: 'كيف أصبحت' assumes you already are in a state.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn hold that no divorce occurs until the wife exercises her choice. Their proof: the husband delegated the divorce entirely to her, so the occurrence itself must depend on her will.
Otherwise, her choice over description would be meaningless in non-consummated marriages.
4
Identify the thamarat al-khilaf
The practical difference appears in two scenarios: (a) when she leaves the session without choosing, and (b) when the marriage has been consummated. Abu Hanifa says the divorce already occurred; the Sahibayn say nothing occurs.
Rejection of the option is treated like leaving.
5
Note the tarjih
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is considered correct ('al-haqq qawluhu'). This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[يتبع] تقع رجعية [ أي طلقة واحدة رجعية بمجرد قوله قبل المشيئة. (ط) أي إذا قال لها: أنت طالق كيف شئت، طلقت واحدة رجعية قبل المشيئة بمجرد قول الزوج، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا تطلق إلا إذا شاءت؛ لأنه فوض التطليق إليها على أي صفة شاءت فلابد من تعليق أصل الطلاق بمشيتها ليكون لها المشيئة في جميع الأحوال أي قبل الدخول وبعده؛ إذ لو لم يتعلق أصل الطلاق لغا قوله: "كيف شئت" في غير المدخول بها؛ لأنها لا تشتغل بإثبات الوصف بعده، ولأبي حنيفة: أن كلمة "كيف" للاستيناف، والوصف يستدعي وجود الأصل، يقال: كيف أصبحت، ويراد السؤال عن الصحة والسقم والعسر واليسر، فإذا كان للاستفهام يقتضي وجود الموصوف، فيقع الطلاق بأصله، ويتعلق وصفه بمشيتها، والحق قوله (مص، فتح) أو ثلاثاً: أي أو شاءت ثلاث تطليقات. ونواه وقع: أي فإن شاءت المرأة واحدة بائنة أو ثلاثاً في المسألة المذكورة وقال الزوج ذلك وقع ما شاءت من البائنة والثلاثة؛ لوجود المطابقة بين إرادته ومشيئتها حتى إذا اختلفت نيته مشيئتها بأن شاءت خلاف ما نوى وقعت واحدة؛ لأن مشيتها لغت، فبقي إيقاع الزوج، ولو لم تحضره النية يجب أن تعتبر مشيئتها جرياً على موجب التخيير، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يقع شيء ما لم تشأ فإن شاءت أوقعت واحدة رجعية أو بائنة أو ثلاثاً بشرط مطابقة إرادته؛ لأنه فوض إليها الطلاق بأي وصف شاءت، وبه قالت الثلاثة وله أنه أوقع الطلاق وخيرها في الوصف، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في موضعين: فيما إذا قامت عن المجلس قبل المشيئة، وفيما إذا كان بعد الدخول، فإنه يقع عنده طلقة رجعية، وعندهما لا يقع شيء، والرد كالقيام رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٣/١] (مص)
And regarding 'kayfa shi'ti': it falls as a revocable divorce, then if she wills an irrevocable or three, it occurs. And regarding 'kam shi'ti' or 'ma shi'ti', she divorces what she wishes therein. i.e., one revocable divorce, and his (the husband's) intention is a kinayah for the session... [continued] It falls as a revocable divorce [i.e., one revocable divorce by his mere statement before the will. (T) i.e., if he said to her: 'You are divorced as you wish', she is divorced one revocable before the will by the mere statement of the husband. This is according to Abu Hanifa. But they (the two) said: She is not divorced unless she wills, because he delegated the divorce to her in whatever manner she wishes, so the divorce itself must be suspended on her will so that she has the option in all situations, i.e., before consummation and after. For if the divorce itself is not suspended, his statement 'kayfa shi'ti' becomes void in the case of a non-consummated wife because she would not bother to prove the description after it. According to Abu Hanifa: the word 'kayfa' is for interrogative, and the description requires the existence of the described; it is said: 'How did you become?' meaning asking about health or sickness, ease or difficulty. Thus, if it is interrogative, it necessitates the existence of the object described, so the divorce occurs in its essence, and its description is suspended on her will. His statement is the truth. (Mus, Fath) Or three: i.e., or she wills three divorces. And he intended it, and it occurs: i.e., if the woman wills one ba'in or three in the mentioned case, and the husband said that, then what she wills of ba'in or three occurs, because of the matching between his will and her wish. But if his intention and her wish differ, such that she wishes contrary to what he intended, then one (revocable) divorce occurs because her wish is void, so the husband's pronunciation remains. And if he had no intention (at the time of pronunciation), her wish must be considered in accordance with the requirement of giving her the choice. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two), nothing occurs unless she wills; if she wills, she effects one revocable, ba'in, or three on condition that it matches his intention, because he delegated the divorce to her in whatever manner she wishes. And the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i?) said that; but he (Abu Hanifa) holds that he pronounced the divorce and gave her choice over the description. The practical consequence of the dispute appears in two places: when she leaves the session before willing, and when it is after consummation. According to him (Abu Hanifa), one revocable divorce occurs; according to them, nothing occurs. And rejection is like leaving. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/253] (Mus)
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This lengthy passage details the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the phrase 'kayfa shi'ti' (as you wish). According to Abu Hanifa, when the husband says 'You are divorced as you wish', an immediate revocable divorce (raj'iyyah) takes place by the mere utterance, before the wife exercises any choice. Then, if she wills a ba'in divorce or three divorces, and it matches the husband's intention, that divorce occurs. If her wish differs from his intention, she has no effect; only the one revocable stands. If the husband had no specific intention, then her wish is considered. The Sahibayn hold the opposite: no divorce occurs until she actually wills; then she may effect one revocable, ba'in, or three, but only if it matches the husband's intention (in the case of ba'in or three). The practical difference (thamarat al-khilaf) appears in two scenarios: (1) if she leaves the session before willing—according to Abu Hanifa, the revocable divorce has already occurred, so she cannot change it; according to the Sahibayn, nothing occurs. (2) If the marriage was consummated or not—Abu Hanifa still holds the immediate revocable occurs; the Sahibayn hold nothing until she wills. The passage also mentions that rejection of the option is like leaving the session. The commentator sides with Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
كيف (kayfa): A word meaning 'as' or 'how', used by the husband; according to Abu Hanifa, it interrogates the manner of an existing thing.
الاستفهام (al-istifham): The interrogative sense of 'kayfa', which Abu Hanifa uses to argue that the divorce exists and its description is questioned.
بائنة (ba'inah): An irrevocable divorce that terminates the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocation (except through a new marriage).
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation; the husband giving the wife authority over some aspect of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One ba'in divorce occurs because she chose it.
B) One revocable divorce occurs because her wish is void and the husband's pronunciation stands.
C) No divorce occurs because their intentions conflict.
D) Three divorces occur by default.

Key Takeaway
The word 'kayfa' necessitates the existence of the divorce immediately; only its description is delegated to the wife.

Ruling for 'Kam Shi'ti' and 'Ma Shi'ti' – Delegation of Divorce Number
Explanation of 'kam' and 'ma' delegation rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates the number of divorces using 'kam' or 'ma' and identify the condition that restricts the wife's choice to the session.

Core Idea
Delegation of divorce with 'kam' or 'ma' requires immediate decision in the same session; otherwise it is void.

This passage concludes the discussion on delegating talaq. It explains the specific ruling when the husband uses indefinite expressions like 'however many you wish' (kam shi'ti) or 'whatever you wish' (ma shi'ti). The key point is that while the wife has full discretion over the number, her choice must be made in the same session or else the delegation lapses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases and their linguistic classification
Recognize that 'kam' is a noun indicating number, so delegation is specifically about the count, while 'ma' is general, covering all possibilities. This linguistic distinction affects the scope of the delegation.
Linguistic analysis is key to understanding the legal implications.
2
Determine the scope of the delegation
The wife may choose one, two, or three divorces. Her will is the trigger; nothing occurs before she wills. This is agreed upon by all (ittifaqan).
The number 'one' is considered a number in fiqh terminology.
3
Pinpoint the temporal restriction
The delegation is a tamlik (transfer of right) that requires an immediate response in the same session. There is no mention of time, so it necessitates an answer like other tamliks.
This is unlike previous delegations that allowed choice over time.
4
Analyze the consequences of leaving the session or rejecting
If the wife leaves the session without choosing, the option becomes void. Similarly, if she explicitly says 'I do not wish' or shows disinterest, she loses the right.
Leaving or rejecting ends the delegation immediately.
5
Confirm the agreed-upon point
There is no intra-Hanafi dispute that nothing occurs before her will. The restriction to the session is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih (disputed with other schools).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تطلق ما شاءت فيه: أي في المجلس سواء شاءت واحدة أو ثنتين أو ثلاثا؛ لأن "كم" اسم للعدد، فكان التفويض في نفس العدد، وليس الواقع إلا بالعدد إذا ذكر، فصار التفويض في نفس الواقع، فلا يقع شيء قبل مشيئتها اتفاقاً، والواحد عدد في اصطلاح الفقهاء، و"ما" عامة تتناول الكل، فإن قامت من المجلس قبل أن تشاء بطل الأمر؛ لأنه أمر واحد، وهو تمليك في الحال، وليس فيه ذكر الوقت، فاقتضى جواباً في المجلس كسائر التمليكات، وكذا إن ردت الأمر بأن قالت لا أشاء فليس لها أن تشاء بعده، وكذا لو أتت بما يدل على الإعراض. (فتح)
She divorces what she wishes therein: i.e., in the session, whether she wishes one, two, or three, because 'kam' is a noun for number, so the delegation is in the number itself, and the occurrence is only by the number when mentioned, so the delegation is in the occurrence itself. Thus nothing occurs before her will by agreement. And 'one' is a number in the terminology of the jurists. And 'ma' is general, encompassing everything. If she leaves the session before she wills, the matter is void, because it is a single matter, which is a tamlik (transfer of ownership) in the present, and it does not contain mention of time, so it requires a response in the session like all other tamliks. Similarly, if she rejects the matter by saying 'I do not will', she has no right to will thereafter. Similarly, if she does something indicating disinterest. (Fath)
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter by explaining the ruling when the husband uses 'kam shi'ti' (however many you wish) or 'ma shi'ti' (whatever you wish). The key point is that 'kam' is a word for number (adad), so the delegation is specifically about the number of divorces. Nothing occurs until she wills, and she may will one, two, or three divorces. The word 'ma' is general and also allows any number. However, this delegation is tied to the session (majlis). If she leaves the session without willing, the option is void. Similarly, if she explicitly refuses (says 'I do not will') or shows disinterest, she loses the option. This is because this delegation is a single tamlik (transfer of right) that requires an immediate response in the same sitting, unlike the earlier delegations that generalized times. The ruling is unanimous (ittifaqan) that nothing occurs before her will, but the restriction to the session is the adopted position.

Key Terms
كم (kam): A word meaning 'how many', used for delegating the number of divorces.
ما (ma): A general word meaning 'whatever', used for delegating the entire right of divorce.
تمليك (tamlik): A legal transfer of a right; here, the husband transfers the right to divorce to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A delegation of divorce using 'kam' or 'ma' is effective only if the wife exercises her choice in the same session; otherwise it becomes void.

Ruling of 'How You Wish' in Delegated Divorce
Explains the ruling when husband says 'ant taliq kayfa shi'ti'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'ant taliq kayfa shi'ti' and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the timing of the divorce.

Core Idea
The word 'kayfa' presumes the existence of the divorce, so it occurs immediately; only its description is delegated to the wife's will.

This passage addresses a specific delegation phrase: when a husband says to his wife 'you are divorced how you will'. Does the divorce happen at once, or is it suspended until she chooses? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagreed, and the passage lays out the linguistic reasoning behind each view. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how Hanafi jurists read grammatical cues to derive rulings in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the issue
Isolate the husband's statement 'ant taliq kayfa shi'ti'. The core legal question is whether the divorce occurs immediately or is conditional on the wife's will.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa: one revocable divorce occurs instantly upon utterance. His proof: the word 'kayfa' is interrogative, presupposing existence of the described (the divorce).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn: no divorce occurs until the wife wills. Their proof: delegation of the description implies delegation of the origin; otherwise her choice is meaningless.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and tarjih
Abu Hanifa responds that the description (kayfa) requires an origin; to avoid nullifying the word, the divorce must exist immediately, and only the quality is delegated. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's ('al-haqq qawlu').
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
If the wife leaves the session without willing, Abu Hanifa says one revocable divorce has already occurred; the Sahibayn say no divorce occurred. In fatwa, Abu Hanifa's view is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حكم المشيئة
حتى تشاء في مجلسها، وفي كيف شِئتِ تقع رجعيّة، فإن شاءت بائنة أو ثلاثاً وقع، وفي كم شئت أو ما شئت تطلق ما شاءت فيه،
أي طلقة واحدة بائنة
ونواه أي الزوج
كناية عن
"أنت طالق شئت"، فينبغي أن يقع في الحال فلم يتعلق، قلنا: يحمل الظرف على الشرط لمناسبة بينهما من حيث أن الظرف يجامع المظروف كما أن الشرط يجامع المشروط، فعند تعذر الظرف حقيقة يصير الشرط مجازاً؛ لأن كلا منهما يفيد ضربا من التأخير وهو أولى من إلغائه أصلاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٢/١](فتح) في مجلسها حتى لو قامت عنه وشاءت في مجلس آخر لا يقع شيء. (ط) وفي :كيف أي في قوله لها أنت طالق كيف إلخ. (ط)
تقع رجعية [ أي طلقة واحدة رجعية بمجرد قوله قبل المشيئة. (ط) أي إذا قال لها: أنت طالق كيف شئت، طلقت واحدة رجعية قبل المشيئة بمجرد قول الزوج، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا تطلق إلا إذا شاءت؛ لأنه فوض التطليق إليها على أي صفة شاءت فلابد من تعليق أصل الطلاق بمشيتها ليكون لها المشيئة في جميع الأحوال أي قبل الدخول وبعده؛ إذ لو لم يتعلق أصل الطلاق لغا قوله: "كيف شئت" في غير المدخول بها؛ لأنها لا تشتغل بإثبات الوصف بعده، ولأبي حنيفة: أن كلمة "كيف" للاستيناف، والوصف يستدعي وجود الأصل، يقال: كيف أصبحت، ويراد السؤال عن الصحة والسقم والعسر واليسر، فإذا كان للاستفهام يقتضي وجود الموصوف، فيقع الطلاق بأصله، ويتعلق وصفه بمشيتها، والحق قوله (مص، فتح) أو ثلاثاً: أي أو شاءت ثلاث تطليقات.
Statement of the ruling of the will: until she wills in her session, and in 'how you will' it falls as revocable, then if she wills irrevocable or three, it occurs. And in 'how many you will' or 'whatever you will' she divorces as many as she wills in it. Meaning: one irrevocable divorce. And he intended, meaning the husband, as a metaphor for 'you are divorced if you will', so it should occur immediately but it did not become conditional. We say: the adverb is interpreted as a condition due to the connection between them in that the adverb encompasses the adverbed as the condition encompasses the conditioned. When the adverbial meaning is impossible in reality, it becomes a condition metaphorically, because each of them provides a type of delay, and that is better than completely disregarding it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/252] (Fath) In her session, so if she stood from it and willed in another session, nothing occurs. (T) And in 'how', that is in his saying to her 'you are divorced how' etc. (T) Falls as revocable: meaning one revocable divorce merely by his saying before the will. (T) That is, if he said to her 'you are divorced how you will', she is divorced one revocable divorce before the will merely by the husband's saying. This is according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: she is not divorced unless she wills, because he delegated the divorce to her in any manner she wills, so it is necessary to suspend the origin of divorce upon her will so that she has the will in all circumstances, i.e., before consummation and after; for if the origin of divorce were not suspended, his saying 'how you will' would be invalid in the case of the non-consummated wife because she does not busy herself with proving the description after it. And for Abu Hanifa: that the word 'how' is for interrogation, and the description requires the existence of the origin. It is said: 'how did you become morning?' and the question is about health and sickness and ease and difficulty. So if it is for interrogation, it requires the existence of the described. So the divorce occurs by its origin, and its description is attached to her will. And the correct is his saying (Misbah, Fath) 'or three': that is, or she wills three divorces.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling when a husband tells his wife 'you are divorced how you will' (kayfa shi'ti). The author first clarifies that if the husband says 'you are divorced when you will' (idha shi'ti), the divorce does not occur immediately but is postponed to her will within the session. However, for 'kayfa shi'ti', the ruling differs. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, one revocable divorce occurs immediately upon his utterance, before the wife chooses anything. The nature of the divorce (whether revocable, irrevocable, or three) is then determined by her will. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that no divorce occurs until she wills, because the husband delegated the entire divorce to her. Abu Hanifa's proof is that 'kayfa' is a word for questioning about the quality of an existing thing, so it presumes the existence of the divorce itself. The description (revocable, irrevocable, three) is then delegated to her choice. The text concludes that if she wills an irrevocable or three, it occurs; if she wills three, it counts as three. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The wife's choice or will regarding the type or number of divorce after the husband's delegation.
كيف (kayfa): An interrogative word meaning 'how', which here presumes the existence of the divorce and questions its quality.
الظرف (al-zarf): The adverb of time or place, here referring to 'when' as an adverbial phrase, which is reinterpreted as a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when a husband says to his wife 'أنت طالق كيف شئت', what happens immediately?
A) No divorce occurs until she wills.
B) One revocable divorce occurs immediately.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs immediately.
D) The divorce is suspended on her will.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, 'kayfa shi'ti' triggers an immediate revocable divorce, with only the description delayed to the wife's choice.
