وكذا إذا علقه بمشيئة من لا تظهر مشيئته لنا كالجن وكالحائط والملائكة يكون تعليقاً أو إبطالاً على الاختلاف المذكور، ولو قال: أنت طالق بمشيئة الله أو بإرادته أو بمحبته أو برضاه لا يقع كقوله: "إن شاء الله"، وإن أضافه إلى العبد كان تمليكاً . منه، فيقتصر على المجلس بأن قال: أنت طالق إن شاء فلان"، ولو قال: بحكمه أو بأمره أو بقضائه أو بإذنه أو بعلمه أو بقدرته يقع في الحال، سواء أضافه إلى الله أو إلى العبد. وإن قال بحرف اللام يقع في الوجوه كلها، سواء أضافه إلى الله أو إلى العبد، وإن ذكر بحرف "في" إن أضافه إلى الله لا يقع في الوجوه كلها إلا في العلم فإنه يقع الطلاق فيه للحال. فالحاصل أن هذه الألفاظ عشرة أربعة منها للتمليك، وهي المشيئة ،والإرادة والمحبة والرضا، وستة ليست للتمليك، وهي الأمر ،والحكم والقضاء والإذن والعلم والقدرة والكل على وجهين إما أن يضاف إلى الله أو إلى العبد، وكل وجه على وجوه ثلاثة: إما أن يكون بالباء أو باللام أو بـ"في"، فكلها باللام تنجيز مطلقاً، وكذلك الستة الأخيرة بالباء، والأربعة الأول بالباء تعليق إن أضيف إلى الله، وتمليك إن أضيف إلى العبد، وكلها بـ"في" إن أضيف
إلى الله تعليق إلا العلم، وإن أضيف إلى العبد فالأربعة الأول تمليك والباقي تعليق. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٥٩/١] وفي إلا ثلاثاً [ أي في قوله: أنت طالق ثلاثا (ع) والأصل في هذا الباب أن الاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الاستثناء عندنا؛ إذ لا فرق بين قول القائل: لفلان علي درهم وبين قوله عشرة إلا تسعة، فصح استثناء البعض من الكل؛ لأنه يبقى التكلم بالبعض غير أن استثناء الكل رجوع لا يبقى بعده شيء ليصير متكلما به، وعند الشافعي: الاستثناء منع الحكم بطريق المعارضة كدليل الخصوص، فإذا قال له علي عشرة إلا خمسة، فعندنا: هي عبارة عن الخمسة وصار اسماً لها وعنده دخلت العشرة كلها ثم خرجت الخمسة بطريق المعارضة كأنه قال: له
على عشرة إلا خمسة فإنها ليست علي يدل عليه قولهم: الاستثناء من النفي إثبات ومن الإثبات نفي. قلنا: ليس في وسعه أن يخرج بعض الحكم بعد ثبوته ولأنه لو كان بطريق المعارضة، لاستوى فيه الكل والبعض كالنسخ ولكان مستقلاً ولما صح في الأخبار ؛ لأن التعارض فيهما يؤدي إلى أن أحدهما كذب أو شبه الكذب، فعلم بذلك أن قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَبِثَ فِيهِمْ أَلْفَ سَنَةٍ إِلَّا خَمْسِينَ عاما (العنكبوت: ١٤) عبارة عن تسع مائة وخمسين، لا أنه سبحانه وتعالى أخبر بأنه لبث فيهم ألف سنة، ثم رجع عنه، وقولهم: "الاستثناء من النفي إثبات ومن الإثبات نفي"
تسامح؛ لأنه لولا الاستثناء لدخل، فمنعه من الدخول فصار كالمخرج بهذا الاعتبار. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦١/١] ثم شرط صحة الاستثناء أن يبقى ما يتكلم به بعد الاستثناء، وهذا إذا كان الاستثناء متصلا بصدر الكلام؛ لأنه مغير لصدره، ففي المسألة الأولى الباقي بعد الاستثناء ثنتان فيقعان وفي الثانية الباقي واحدة فتقع، وفي الثالثة لم يبق بعد الاستثناء ما يتكلم به فيبطل الاستثناء ويقع الثلاث، بخلاف العطف حيث لا يشترط فيه أن يكون موصولا منفصلا لكونه غير مغير. (مستخلص) ثلاث: أي يقع ثلاث؛ لأن الاستثناء المستغرق ،باطل، فإنه إنكار بعد الإقرار. (ع)
ويصح
Book of Divorce. Rulings of Suspension. And in 'You are divorced three times except one, two occur; and except two, one occurs; and except three, three occur. That is, in his statement to her... similarly, if he suspends it on the will of one whose will is not apparent to us, like the jinn, the wall, and the angels, it is suspension or nullification according to the mentioned difference. And if he says: You are divorced by the will of Allah, or by His intention, or by His love, or by His pleasure, it does not occur, like his saying 'If Allah wills'. And if he adds it to the slave, it is a delegation (tamlik). So it is restricted to the session if he says: You are divorced if so-and-so wills. And if he says: by His decree, or by His command, or by His judgment, or by His permission, or by His knowledge, or by His power, it occurs immediately, whether he adds it to Allah or to the slave. And if he says with the letter 'lam', it occurs in all cases, whether added to Allah or to the slave. And if he mentions with the letter 'fi', if he adds it to Allah, it does not occur in all cases except in (the case of) knowledge, for divorce occurs therein immediately. So the upshot is that these expressions are ten: four are for delegation (tamlik): will, intention, love, pleasure; and six are not for delegation: command, decree, judgment, permission, knowledge, power. And all are in two aspects: either added to Allah or to the slave, and each aspect has three forms: either with ba', or with lam, or with fi. So all with lam are immediate (tanjiz) absolutely. Similarly the last six with ba'. And the first four with ba' are suspension (ta'liq) if added to Allah, and delegation if added to the slave. And all with fi, if added to Allah, are suspension except knowledge; if added to the slave, then the first four are delegation and the rest are suspension. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 259/1] And in 'except three' i.e., in his statement: You are divorced three times (this is the matn). And the principle in this chapter is that exception is speaking of the remainder after the exception according to us; for there is no difference between one's saying: So-and-so owes me a dirham and his saying: ten except nine. So exception of a part from the whole is valid, because speaking of the part remains, except that exception of the whole is a retraction, after which nothing remains to be spoken of. According to al-Shafi'i: exception is prevention of the ruling by way of opposition, like a specific evidence. So if he says: He owes me ten except five, according to us it is an expression for five and becomes a name for it; according to him, the entire ten entered, then the five exit by way of opposition, as if he said: He owes me ten except five, which is not owed. This is supported by their saying: exception from negation is affirmation, and from affirmation is negation. We say: It is not within his capacity to remove part of a ruling after its establishment, and because if it were by way of opposition, then whole and part would be equal, like abrogation, and it would be independent, and it would not be valid in reports, because opposition therein leads to one of them being a lie or resembling a lie. Thus it is known that Allah's statement: "So he remained among them a thousand years except fifty years" (al-Ankabut 29:14) is an expression for nine hundred and fifty, not that He informed that he remained a thousand years and then retracted. And their saying 'exception from negation is affirmation and from affirmation is negation' is a loose expression because, were it not for the exception, it would have entered, so he prevented its entry, so it becomes as if excluded by this consideration. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 261/1] Then the condition for validity of exception is that there remains something to be spoken of after the exception, and this is when the exception is connected to the beginning of the speech because it changes the beginning. So in the first case, the remainder after the exception is two, so they occur; in the second, the remainder is one, so it occurs; in the third, nothing remains after the exception to be spoken of, so the exception becomes invalid and all three occur, unlike conjunction (atf) where it is not required to be connected or separated because it does not change (the speech). (Mukhtasar) 'Three': i.e., three occur, because all-encompassing exception is invalid, as it is a denial after affirmation. (Matn) And it is valid.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of conditional divorce (ta'liq) and exception (istithna) in the Hanafi school. It begins by presenting the matn's ruling on exception in a triple divorce pronouncement. The author then expands on the classification of ten expressions used in conditional divorce, detailing how they function depending on whether they are attached to Allah or to a slave, and whether they use the prepositions ba, lam, or fi. The core Hanafi principle is that exception is a way of speaking about the remainder after exclusion, not a withdrawal of the original statement. The passage includes a detailed dispute with al-Shafi'i, who views exception as a preventive opposition. The Hanafi argument relies on Quranic usage (Surah al-Ankabut, 29:14) and logical consistency. Finally, the conditions for valid exception are given: the exception must be connected to the beginning of the statement, and there must remain something to be spoken of; otherwise, the exception is invalid (as in excepting all three from triple divorce).

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): Linguistically exclusion; legally, a statement that makes a part of the subject remain as spoken of after exclusion.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a divorce on the occurrence of a condition or will of another.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Delegating the authority to divorce to the wife by giving her the choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'You are divorced three times except three', how many divorces occur?
A) Zero
B) One
C) Three
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Exception in divorce defines the net number of divorces intended; an all-encompassing exception is invalid and the full number applies.

Inheritance after Divorce in Terminal Illness: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Explains rulings of divorce by a terminally ill person and its effect on inheritance, with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a woman divorced irrevocably during her husband's terminal illness inherits from him, identify the conditions, and evaluate the evidence of opposing schools.

Core Idea
A sick husband's irrevocable divorce does not deprive his wife of inheritance if she is still in iddah at his death.

This lesson tackles a critical juncture where family law meets inheritance: what happens when a terminally ill husband divorces his wife? The Hanafi school protects the wife's right by looking beyond the divorce to the husband's possible evasion, using the concept of 'al-farr' (the evader). We will dissect the ruling, the conditions, and the significant inter-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Define the Context
Identify the key scenario: a terminally ill husband (marid marad al-mawt) divorces his wife. Note that the divorce itself is valid, but its effect on inheritance is conditional.
The definition of 'sick' differs for men and women.
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi ruling: the wife inherits if the husband dies during her iddah; she does not inherit if the iddah has ended. Distinguish between revocable (always inherits) and irrevocable (only during iddah) divorce.
In revocable divorce, marriage persists, so inheritance is mutual.
3
Identify the Opposing Schools
Compare with Shafi'i (no inheritance ever), Maliki (always inherits), and Hanbali (inherits until remarriage). Understand their key evidence.
The Shafi'i view is based on complete dissolution of marriage; Maliki on Uthman's practice.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Evidence
Examine the dalil: Caliph Umar's letter to Judge Shuraih instructing inheritance for the 'evader's wife' during iddah. This is a qawl sahabi (Companion's statement) that Hanafis consider binding.
The concept of 'farr' (evasion) justifies overriding the apparent dissolution.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Work through a practical scenario to see the consequence: a sick man irrevocably divorces his wife, then dies during her iddah. Hanafi: she inherits; Shafi'i: she does not.
Intra-Hanafi variation: if divorce happened before consummation, wife does not inherit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
٤٤٥
باب طلاق المريض
باب طلاق المريض
أي في بيان أحكامه
طلقها رجعيّاً أو بائناً أو ثلاثاً في مرضه ومات في عدتها ورثت، وبعدها لا.
بغیر رضاها
أي في عدة المرأة
العدة
باب طلاق المريض [تفسيره في حق الزوج أن يكون بحيث يعجز عن قضاء مصالحه خارج البيت وفي حق المرأة أن تعجز عن المصالح الداخلة. [Its interpretation regarding the husband is that he be incapable of fulfilling his needs outside the house; regarding the woman, that she be incapable of internal affairs. (T)] Since sickness is an accidental condition, its explanation is deferred from the original ruling, which is health, and the appropriateness between the two chapters is that suspension is a pronouncement from one perspective and not from another, considering the existence of the condition or its absence; similarly, the divorce of the sick person occurs from one perspective as in other rulings, and does not occur from another perspective as in the right of inheritance. And the sick person is also called 'al-farr' (evader). They differed in its interpretation: some said he is one who cannot stand by his own strength unless someone makes him stand; some said he is bedridden and cannot fulfill his needs except with difficulty, and the prevalent condition is death. The correct view is that if he can manage his needs inside the house but cannot manage them outside, he is not sick with a mortal illness. And the woman, if she cannot climb to the roof, and the better view is that she is incapable of internal affairs, she is sick; otherwise not. (Mukhtasar, Miskin) 'He divorced her' means the sick man divorced his free Muslim wife. (T) 'Revocably' means a revocable divorce, even if she is divorced. (T) 'During his sickness' is a condition for the irrevocable divorce because in revocable the marriage persists, hence he inherits from her if she dies. (Matn) That is, if a man divorces his wife irrevocably during his sickness and dies during her iddah, she inherits; and in revocable, she inherits absolutely if he dies while she is in iddah, and so he inherits from her if she dies, unlike the irrevocable because the cause is the marriage and it has ceased, so she should not inherit from him just as he does not inherit from her. However, if he becomes an evader (farr) by divorcing her after her right to his property has attached, and she was at the time of divorce among those who inherit from him (both free and of same religion), his intention is thwarted. Unlike if she was disbeliever and he Muslim, or both were slaves or one of them at divorce time, then later the impediment is removed—she does not inherit because her right to his property was not attached at divorce, so he is not an evader. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 261/1] 'She inherits' i.e., the woman from him due to the persistence of marriage. (Matn) 'After it, no' i.e., she does not inherit absolutely, whether she remarries or not; similarly, she does not inherit if he divorced her before consummation. (T) That is, if the man dies after the expiry of her iddah, she does not inherit. Al-Shafi'i in the new view says: there is no inheritance for the irrevocably divorced woman absolutely, due to the cessation of marriage in all its consequences. Malik says: she inherits absolutely, i.e., before and after the iddah, due to the statement of Uthman regarding Tumadir bint al-Asbagh, wife of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf: 'The Book of Allah is the evader from whom his intention is thwarted, without distinction.' And according to Ahmad: she inherits before marrying another husband; after it, no. Our evidence is that Umar wrote to Shuraih: 'Let the wife of the evader inherit as long as she is in the iddah.' And Tumadir was in her iddah. They differed about one who remained ill for more than two years then died, and then she gave birth after his death in less than six months: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she does not inherit; according to Abu Yousuf's principle: the irrevocably divorced woman, if she gives birth after more than two years, her iddah ends with it in his view, because the pregnancy is a new occurrence? She inherits, and this is based on the position that she is in iddah from fornication? Hence the lineage is not established from him, but we are certain of the uterus being free after delivery, so the iddah ends with it, and according to the two, it is considered as -
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the divorce of a terminally ill person. It first defines 'sick' (al-marid) as one unable to manage external needs, and for a woman, unable to manage internal affairs. The core ruling: if a sick husband divorces his wife irrevocably and dies during her iddah, she inherits; after the iddah, she does not. For revocable divorce, she inherits even if the divorce was in health because the marriage persists. However, the husband may be considered an 'evader' (farr) if he divorces to deprive her of inheritance, in which case his scheme is thwarted. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i denies inheritance absolutely; Imam Malik grants it absolutely; Imam Ahmad grants it until she remarries. The Hanafi evidence relies on the practice of Caliph Umar writing to Judge Shuraih to allow inheritance for the evader's wife during iddah. Finally, an intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: if the husband remains ill for over two years, then dies, and the wife gives birth less than six months after his death, Abu Yusuf rules she inherits because her iddah ends only with delivery, while Muhammad and Abu Yusuf (in another view) consider the pregnancy from fornication, so iddah is not established.

Key Terms
المريض مرض الموت (al-marid marad al-mawt): The terminally ill person: one unable to manage needs outside the home; for a woman, unable to manage internal affairs.
الفار (al-farr): The evader: a sick husband who divorces so as to deprive his wife of inheritance.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death, during which the woman cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a woman divorced irrevocably by her terminally ill husband if he dies after her iddah has expired?
A) She inherits because the divorce was during illness
B) She does not inherit because the iddah has ended
C) She inherits only if she hasn't remarried
D) She does not inherit because irrevocable divorce severs inheritance

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi judge must ascertain the iddah status at the time of the husband's death to determine inheritance, applying the farr concept only when the wife's eligibility was intact at divorce.

Inheritance After Divorce in Fatal Illness: The Hanafi Middle Ground
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on inheritance after fatal illness divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance for a woman divorced in her husband's fatal illness and compare it to the positions of other major schools.

Core Idea
A wife divorced in fatal illness inherits only during 'iddah — a balance between marriage preservation and legal stratagem prevention.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a critical intersection of divorce and inheritance law: what happens when a husband divorces his wife in his terminal illness, possibly to deprive her of inheritance? The Hanafi school navigates between the extremes of total inheritance denial and unconditional inheritance, anchoring its ruling on the 'iddah period. We will examine the school's proof from the precedent of Umar (ra) and contrast it with the views of other imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify key actors and terms
Focus on 'al-far' (the one who divorces to disinherit) and distinguish between revocable (raj'i) and irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. Note that the 'iddah period is central.
Al-far is defined in the passage as someone who divorces in fatal illness to deprive inheritance.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The woman inherits if the husband dies within her 'iddah; not after. This applies to both revocable and irrevocable divorces, though the reasoning differs.
For revocable divorce, marriage persists; for irrevocable, it is a far (fraud) prevention.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
Umar's letter to Shuraih: 'Give inheritance to the woman of al-far as long as she is in her waiting period.' The case of Tamadir supports this.
The dalil is an athar (companion precedent), classified as zanni in transmission but qati in meaning.
4
Compare with other schools
Shafi'i (new): no inheritance; Malik: always; Ahmad: until remarriage. Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
Each school relies on different hadiths or qiyas.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify how the rulings differ in practice: Hanafi limits to 'iddah, Maliki extends indefinitely, Shafi'i denies completely, Hanbali adds condition of no remarriage.
This highlights the importance of textual interpretation in fiqh.

Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The passage begins by defining a terminally ill person (marid mawt) as one unable to conduct external affairs, and for a woman, unable to handle internal chores. The basic ruling is provided: if a husband divorces his wife (revocably, irrevocably, or three times) during his terminal illness and dies during her 'iddah, she inherits; if he dies after her 'iddah, she does not. The text clarifies that in revocable divorce the marriage persists, so inheritance is mutual. In irrevocable divorce, the marriage ends, but the wife inherits if the divorce was a 'frivolous' (far) attempt to deprive her. The definition of 'far' is discussed. The passage then presents the disagreement among schools: Shafi'i (in his new opinion) denies inheritance absolutely; Malik grants it even after 'iddah based on a hadith of Uthman; Ahmad grants it until remarriage. The Hanafi position relies on Umar's letter to Shuraih limiting inheritance to the 'iddah period. This establishes the Hanafi view as the middle ground.

Key Terms
الفار (al-fār): A person who divorces his wife in his fatal illness to deprive her of inheritance.
المبانة (al-mubānah): The wife who has been divorced with a final (ba'in) divorce.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Never, even if he dies during 'iddah
D) Only if she did not initiate the divorce

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a woman divorced in her husband's fatal illness inherits if he dies within her 'iddah, based on Umar's letter, which limits inheritance to that period.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Prolonged Illness and Post-Death Birth
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance when birth occurs after death in prolonged illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf on inheritance when a divorced woman gives birth within six months of her ex-husband's death after a prolonged illness, and explain the underlying principle regarding 'iddah and pregnancy attribution.

Core Idea
When birth occurs after death within six months, inheritance hinges on whether the pregnancy is attributed to adultery or a remarriage.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement about a divorced woman's inheritance when her ex-husband dies after a prolonged illness and she gives birth shortly after his death. The scholars differ on whether the birth indicates that her 'iddah had not ended, thus entitling her to inheritance. The dispute reveals deeper principles about how presumptions of pregnancy origin affect legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the two positions
Identify the exact factual scenario: a man fatally ill for over two years, dies, and his divorced wife gives birth within six months. Note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule she does not inherit, while Abu Yusuf rules she does.
2
Identify the underlying principle
Determine how the birth relates to the 'iddah (waiting period). The key question is whether the pregnancy is attributed to the first husband (from adultery) or to a remarriage after the 'iddah ended.
3
Trace each side's reasoning
Explain Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the birth within six months shows the 'iddah was ongoing because the pregnancy, if from adultery, would end the 'iddah only upon delivery. Then explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the pregnancy is attributed to a second marriage to avoid harm to the child, so the 'iddah ended before death.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the woman does not inherit; according to Abu Yusuf, she does. This difference affects financial rights and lineage determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في من دام به المرض أكثر من سنتين ثم مات، ثم جاءت بولد بعد موته لأقل من ستة أشهر، فعندهما لا ترث، وعند أبي يوسف: ترث، وهو مبني على أصل، وهو: أن المبانة إذا جاءت بولد لأكثر من سنتين تنقضي به العدة عنده؛ لأن الحمل حادث من زنا، ولهذا لا يثبت نسبه منه، لكن تيقنا ببراءة الرحم بعد وضعه فتنقضي به العدة، وعندهما يحمل على أن الحمل من زوج تزوجته بعد انقضاء عدتها من الأول؛ لأن في حمله على الزنا إضرار بالولد، فلا يحمل عليه، ولا يقبل قولها: إنه من الزنا، فتعين أن عدتها قد انقضت قبل موته فلا ترث [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/١]
They differed regarding one whose illness lasted more than two years then he died, then she gave birth after his death within less than six months: according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) she does not inherit, and according to Abu Yusuf she inherits, and it is based on a principle, which is that the irrevocably divorced woman, if she gives birth after more than two years, the 'iddah ends thereby in his view, because the pregnancy is from adultery, and therefore lineage is not established from him, but we are certain of the clearing of the womb after its delivery, so the 'iddah ends with it. According to the two, the pregnancy is attributed to a husband she married after the expiry of her 'iddah from the first, because attributing it to adultery harms the child, so it is not so attributed, and her statement that it is from adultery is not accepted, so it is established that her 'iddah had expired before his death, so she does not inherit. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 262/1]
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific scenario: a man is terminally ill for over two years, dies, and his divorced wife gives birth within six months of his death. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule that she does not inherit, while Abu Yusuf says she does. The reasoning hinges on how the pregnancy affects the 'iddah. Abu Yusuf holds that if the pregnancy appears after more than two years from the divorce, it must be from adultery, so it ends the 'iddah upon delivery; but here the birth is within six months, so the 'iddah had not ended, thus she inherits. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that the pregnancy is assumed to be from a second marriage after the 'iddah expired, as attributing it to adultery harms the child. Therefore, her 'iddah ended before his death, and she does not inherit. This illustrates how presumptions about childbirth affect inheritance rights.

Key Terms
المبانة (al-mubānah): The wife who has been divorced with a final (ba'in) divorce.
براءة الرحم (barā'at al-rahim): Certification that the womb is empty of pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The woman inherits because the birth shows the 'iddah had not ended
B) The woman does not inherit because the pregnancy is attributed to a second marriage
C) The woman inherits only if the birth occurs within two years of divorce
D) The woman does not inherit regardless of the birth

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi dispute, the presumption of pregnancy's origin (adultery vs remarriage) is decisive for inheritance after prolonged illness.

Inheritance after Wife-Initiated Divorce: Intra-Hanafi Rules
Intra-Hanafi rulings on inheritance after wife-initiated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on inheritance when a wife initiates irrevocable divorce, revocable divorce, and when the husband acknowledges a debt or bequest, and identify the intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
A wife who asks for irrevocable divorce forfeits inheritance; revocable divorce preserves it; acknowledgment during illness raises suspicion.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary examines how the wife's role in divorce affects her inheritance rights. The core principles: voluntary forfeiture, the nature of revocable vs. irrevocable divorce, and the suspicion of collusion (tuhma) in fiduciary transactions during fatal illness. We will work through the scenarios presented, noting the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Classify divorce types
Distinguish between irrevocable divorce (ba'in) initiated by the wife's request, khul', or delegation, and revocable divorce (raj'i) where she asks for a revocable divorce. Identify the inheritance consequence for each.
Irrevocable: no inheritance. Revocable: inheritance preserved if three talags given.
2
Analyze the acknowledgment scenario
When the husband, after divorcing at her request or by mutual agreement, acknowledges a debt or bequeaths to her, examine the suspicion of collusion. Abu Hanifa: lesser of acknowledgment or inheritance. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: full amount in mutual agreement; lesser in wife's request. Zufar: full amount always.
The 'iddah period is key: if expired, suspicion reduces.
3
Trace the reasoning of each Imam
Abu Hanifa: suspicion (tuhma) persists in both cases because the divorce could have occurred in illness. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: mutual agreement and expired 'iddah make her a stranger, removing suspicion. Zufar: inheritance not nullified by her request.
The majority (the three) agree on the lesser in wife's request case.
4
Apply to other separation grounds
If separation is due to the wife's aversion, impotence, option of puberty, or emancipation, she also does not inherit. But if she initiates these while sick, the husband inherits from her.
Distinguish the case of 'farah' where the husband inherits from a sick wife who initiated separation.

(ع) ومضي: أي ومضى من الزوج إقرار بدين أو وصية لزوجته التي أبانها بأمرها ثم مات. (ط) أن الحمل من زوج تزوجته بعد انقضاء عدتها من الأول؛ لأن في حمله على الزنا إضرار بالولد، فلا يحمل عليه، ولا يقبل قولها: إنه من الزنا، فتعين أن عدتها قد انقضت قبل موته فلا ترث [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/١] بتفويضه : أي بتفويض الزوج إليها بأن قال لها اختاري نفسك. (ع) لم ترث لأنها رضيت لإسقاط حقها بخلاف ما إذا طلقت نفسها ثلاثاً، فأجاز حيث ترث؛ لأن المبطل للإرث إجازته، وبخلاف النسب؛ لأنه لا يقبل الإبطال، وقال مالك: لها الميراث في جميع ذلك. ولو فارقته بسبب الحب والعنة وخيار البلوغ والعتق لم ترث؛ لأن الفرقة من ومضي (ع) قبلها. ولو وجدت هذه الأشياء منها وهي مريضة ورثها الزوج لكونها .فارة [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/١] (فتح) وفي طلقني: أي في قول المرأة لزوجها. (ع) ورثت لأن الطلاق الرجعي لا يزيل النكاح، ولهذا يحل له وطؤها، ولا تحرم به الميراث فلم تكن بسؤالها إياه راضية ببطلان حقها، وكذا لو طلقها واحدة بائنة وفيه خلاف الشافعي. رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/١] لكن لو قالت طلقني بائنة، فأبانها لا ترث كما مر. (مسكين) وإن أبانها إلخ: ولو قالت: طلقني بائناً أو أبانها لا ترث. (ط) في الصحة: أي البينونة كانت في الصحة. العدة: أي وتصادقا أيضاً على انقضاء العدة بأن قال لها في مرضه إن الطلاق البائن كان في صحتي وقد مضت عدتك فصدقته. (ط ، ع) فلها الأقل منه [ أي مما أقر أو أوصى لها به (ط)] أي إذا أبان المريض امرأته بأمرها، أو تصادقا على أنه أبانها في الصحة ومضي عدتها، ثم أقر لها بدين، أو أوصى لها بوصية فلها الأقل مما أقر به أو أوصى لها إرثها عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الأصل أن قول الزوج المتهم ،مردود ، وأن الوصية للوارث باطلة فيحتمل أن الزوجين إنما تواضعا على ذلك ليصح إقراره ووصيته لها، ويحتمل أن قد كان طلقها في الصحة حقيقة وانقضت عدتها، أو أبانها في المرض؛ لأن غرضه الإقرار أو الوصية فيثبت الأقل، ولا تثبت الزيادة على الأقل نفياً للتهمة، وهذا في صورة التصادق عنده وقالا: يجوز إقراره ووصيته. ومن أبانها بأمرها، فلها الأقل في قولهم جميعاً خلافاً لزفر، فإن عنده لها ما أقر أو أوصي؛ لأن الميراث لا يبطل بسؤالها وزال المانع من صحة الإقرار والوصية، ولهما في التصادق أنهما لما تصادقا على الطلاق وانقضاء العدة صارت أجنبية عنه، فانعدمت التهمة. ألا ترى أنه تقبل شهادته لها، ويجوز دفع الزكاة إليها، بخلاف صورة سؤالها الطلاق؛ لأن العدة باقية وهي سبب التهمة، ولا عدة في صورة التصادق فافترقا ولأبي حنيفة: في المسألتين: أن التهمة قائمة للاحتمال الذي ذكرنا والمواضعة عادة في حق الزكاة والشهادة فلا تهمة فيهما، وقالت الثلاثة مثل قول الصاحبين. (مستخلص، عيني)
And if he divorces her irrevocably at her command, or she obtains khul', or she chooses herself by his delegation, she does not inherit. And in the case of 'divorce me revocably' so he divorced her three times, she inherits. And if he divorces her irrevocably at her command during his illness, or they mutually agree that it took place in health... that is, a revocable divorce, the husband, that is three talags, that is the husband divorced his wife irrevocably... the waiting period, then he acknowledged a debt or bequeathed to her, she gets the lesser of the two. And it passed, meaning the husband acknowledged a debt or bequest for his wife whom he had divorced irrevocably at her command, then he died. (A) 'And it passed' meaning the husband's acknowledgment of a debt or bequest for his wife whom he had divorced irrevocably at her command, then he died. (T) That the pregnancy is from a husband she married after the expiry of her 'iddah from the first, because attributing it to adultery harms the child, so it is not so attributed, and her statement that it is from adultery is not accepted, so it is established that her 'iddah had expired before his death, so she does not inherit. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 262/1] 'By his delegation' meaning by the husband delegating to her by saying 'choose yourself'. (A) She does not inherit because she consented to the forfeiture of her right, unlike if she divorced herself three times and he permitted it, where she inherits, because the invalidator of inheritance is his permission, and unlike lineage because it cannot be invalidated. Malik said: She inherits in all that. And if she separated from him due to dislike, impotence, option of puberty, or emancipation, she does not inherit, because the separation is from her side. (A) 'Before her' ...? And if these things occurred from her while she was sick, the husband inherits from her because she is a 'fārah'. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 262/1] (Fath) And in 'divorce me': that is, the woman's statement to her husband. (A) She inherits because revocable divorce does not remove the marriage, therefore intercourse is permissible for him, and inheritance is not prohibited by it, so she was not by asking him consenting to the nullification of her right. Similarly, if he divorced her with one irrevocable divorce, and there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 262/1] But if she said 'divorce me irrevocably', and he divorced her irrevocably, she does not inherit as previously mentioned. (Miskin) 'And if he divorces her irrevocably' etc.: and if she said 'divorce me irrevocably' or he divorced her irrevocably, she does not inherit. (T) 'In health' meaning the irrevocability occurred in health. (T, A) She gets the lesser of the two [i.e., of what he acknowledged or bequeathed to her (T)] That is, if the sick man divorces his wife irrevocably at her command, or they mutually agree that he divorced her irrevocably in health and her 'iddah passed, then he acknowledges a debt or bequeaths to her, she gets the lesser of what he acknowledged or bequeathed as her inheritance according to Abu Hanifa, because the principle is that the husband's statement is rejected due to suspicion, and a bequest to an heir is invalid. It is possible that the couple agreed upon that to make his acknowledgment and bequest valid for her, and it is possible that he truly divorced her in health and her 'iddah expired, or he divorced her irrevocably during illness, because his purpose is acknowledgment or bequest, so the lesser is established, and the increase over the lesser is not established to remove suspicion. This is in the case of mutual agreement according to him. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: his acknowledgment and bequest are valid. And in the case of divorcing at her command, she gets the lesser according to all of them, contrary to Zufar, for according to him she gets what he acknowledged or bequeathed, because inheritance is not nullified by her asking, and the obstacle to the validity of acknowledgment and bequest is removed. For the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), in the case of mutual agreement, since they agreed on the divorce and expiry of the 'iddah, she becomes a stranger to him, so the suspicion is removed. Do you not see that her testimony is accepted for him, and it is permissible to pay zakat to her, unlike the case of her asking for divorce, because the 'iddah remains and is the cause of suspicion. And there is no 'iddah in the case of mutual agreement, so they differ. According to Abu Hanifa, in both cases the suspicion persists due to the possibility we mentioned, and collusion is customary regarding zakat and testimony, so there is no suspicion there. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said like the two companions' statement. (Mukhtasar, Ayni)
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage covers various scenarios where inheritance is affected by the wife's role in the divorce. The first set: if the wife requests the divorce (ba'in), obtains khula', or uses delegation (tawfid), she does not inherit because she voluntarily forfeited her right. However, if she asks for a revocable divorce (طلب طلاق رجعي) and the husband gives three talags, she inherits because revocable divorce does not break the marriage. The second major issue is when the husband, after divorcing his wife at her request, acknowledges a debt or makes a bequest to her. If they mutually agree that the divorce occurred in health and her 'iddah has passed, Abu Hanifa says she takes the lesser of the acknowledged/bequeathed amount or her inheritance, due to possible collusion; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the acknowledgment valid. If the divorce was at her request during illness, Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad all agree she takes the lesser, except Zufar who grants the full amount. The passage also mentions that separation due to the wife's aversion (ubt) or other grounds from her side (e.g., impotence, option of puberty, emancipation) deprives her of inheritance, but if she initiates these while sick, the husband inherits from her as she is a 'farah' (female far).

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation by the husband to the wife to choose divorce.
الخلع (al-khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation to the husband.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion of collusion between spouses to circumvent inheritance law.
العنة (al-'anna): Impotence of the husband.
خيار البلوغ (khiyār al-bulūgh): Option of puberty: the right of a minor married by someone other than father/grandfather to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway: The wife's role in divorce affects inheritance; revocable divorce preserves inheritance, irrevocable initiated by her forfeits it, and acknowledgment during illness is subject to suspicion leading to the lesser rule.

When a Wife Causes Her Own Divorce, She Does Not Inherit
Ruling on inheritance when wife initiates separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a wife who initiates divorce inherits, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
A wife who causes her own divorce forfeits inheritance — her consent to end the marriage waives her right.

This passage presents a clear Hanafi principle: if the wife is the active cause of the divorce, she loses her inheritance from her husband even if he dies during the idda. The rationale is rooted in consent — by seeking the end of the marriage, she implicitly waives her right to inherit. We will examine the types of wife-initiated divorce covered, the exceptional case, and the Maliki disagreement to understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of wife-initiated divorce
The text lists three scenarios: wife asks for divorce (أمرها), khula' (اختلعت), and delegation (تفويض). List each and note that all result in no inheritance.
Also note the additional causes from her side: imprisonment, impotence, option of puberty, emancipation.
2
Understand the rationale: consent to abrogation
The phrase 'رضيت لإسقاط حقها' (she consented to abrogation of her right) is key. The wife's active role shows she waived her inheritance right.
Contrast with cases where the husband initiates divorce.
3
Contrast with the exception: husband's permission
The text gives an exception: if she divorces herself three times and the husband allows it, she inherits. Why? Because the invalidating factor is his permission, not her action.
This sharpens the rule: the wife's consent must be the direct cause of the divorce.
4
Note the Maliki opposition
Imam Malik holds that she inherits in all these cases. The text does not give his proof, but it implies he views the marriage bond as still intact until idda ends.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both views have internal logic.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In a Hanafi jurisdiction, the wife does not inherit; in a Maliki jurisdiction, she does. This affects estate distribution and fatwa.
Understand that the ruling is tied to the wife's role as initiator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أبانها بأمرها، أو اختلعت منه، أو اختارت نفسها بتفويضه لم ترث. لم ترث لأنها رضيت لإسقاط حقها بخلاف ما إذا طلقت نفسها ثلاثاً، فأجاز حيث ترث؛ لأن المبطل للإرث إجازته، وبخلاف النسب؛ لأنه لا يقبل الإبطال، وقال مالك: لها الميراث في جميع ذلك. ولو فارقته بسبب الحب والعنة وخيار البلوغ والعتق لم ترث؛ لأن الفرقة من قبلها.
And if he divorces her by her command, or she khula' from him, or she chooses herself by his delegation, she does not inherit. She does not inherit because she consented to the abrogation of her right, unlike the case where she divorces herself three times and he permits it, where she inherits; because the invalidator of inheritance is his permission, and unlike lineage because it cannot be abrogated. Malik said: She has inheritance in all of that. And if she separates from him due to imprisonment, impotence, option of puberty, or emancipation, she does not inherit because the separation is from her side.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding inheritance when a wife is the cause of the divorce. The rule is clear: if the wife asks for the divorce, or initiates khula', or uses a delegation (tafwid) to choose herself, she does not inherit from her husband if he dies during the idda. The reason given is that she willingly consented to the abrogation of her right to inheritance. This is contrasted with the case where she divorces herself three times with the husband's permission—there she inherits because the husband's permission is what invalidates inheritance, not her action. The passage also cites Imam Malik's opposing view that she inherits in all these cases. Additionally, any separation originating from the wife—such as her choosing faskh due to the husband's imprisonment, impotence, or exercising her option of puberty or emancipation (if she was a slave)—also deprives her of inheritance because the separation is from her side. This highlights the Hanafi principle that inheritance is tied to the marital bond, and if the wife severs it, she loses her right.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife to choose herself (talaq).
خلع (khula'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually financial).
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): Option of puberty: a minor married by a guardian has the right to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A wife who directly causes the dissolution of her marriage loses her inheritance from her husband, because her consent to end the marriage constitutes a waiver of her right.

Inheritance After a Wife's Request for Divorce: The Hanafi Distinction
Ruling that wife inherits when she asks for divorce without specifying bain, with reasoning and a Shafi'i difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a wife asks for divorce without specifying 'bain', and distinguish the case where she specifies 'ba'in'.

Core Idea
A wife who simply asks for divorce inherits if the divorce is revocable, because her request does not imply consent to lose her rights.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a subtle but important point in family law: does a wife's request for divorce affect her inheritance rights? The Hanafi school draws a clear line based on the type of divorce and the specificity of her request. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying inheritance rules in cases of divorce initiated by the wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine what the wife said (with or without specifying 'ba'in') and what kind of divorce the husband gave (revocable or irrevocable).
The key variable is whether the wife used the word 'bain' or not.
2
Understand the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: if she says simply 'طلقني' and he gives a revocable divorce (one or three), she inherits. If she says 'طلقني بائنة' and he gives a ba'in divorce, she does not inherit.
The ruling hinges on the nature of the divorce (raj'i vs ba'in) and the wife's intention inferred from her words.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The reason is that a revocable divorce (talq raj'i) does not completely end the marriage contract; the husband can take her back, and inheritance rights remain. The wife's general request does not show consent to lose her inheritance.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the definition of raj'i talaq.
4
Apply the distinction
Apply the same rule to the case of one ba'in divorce: if she didn't specify 'bain', she still inherits (Hanafi view), contrary to al-Shafi'i. If she specified 'bain', she does not inherit.
The distinction is based on her explicit words, not the outcome of the divorce.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i differs on the case of one ba'in divorce given in response to an unspecified request. In the Shafi'i school, a ba'in divorce, even if not requested explicitly, severs inheritance.
This ikhtilaf shows how different usul lead to different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي طلقني: أي في قول المرأة لزوجها. ورثت لأن الطلاق الرجعي لا يزيل النكاح، ولهذا يحل له وطؤها، ولا تحرم به الميراث فلم تكن بسؤالها إياه راضية ببطلان حقها، وكذا لو طلقها واحدة بائنة وفيه خلاف الشافعي. لكن لو قالت طلقني بائنة، فأبانها لا ترث كما مر. ولو قالت: طلقني بائناً أو أبانها لا ترث.
And in 'طلقني': i.e., in the woman's statement to her husband. She inherits because revocable divorce does not remove the marriage, therefore intercourse is permissible for him, and inheritance is not prohibited by it, so she was not, by asking him, consenting to the invalidation of her right. Similarly, if he divorced her with one irrevocable divorce, and there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i. But if she said 'divorce me irrevocably', and he divorces her irrevocably, she does not inherit as before. And if she said: 'divorce me irrevocably' or he divorces her irrevocably, she does not inherit.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage clarifies a crucial distinction. When a wife simply says 'طلقني' (divorce me) without specifying the type or number of divorces, and the husband responds by divorcing her three times (which is a revocable set of three), she inherits if he dies during the idda. The reason is that revocable divorce does not sever the marriage completely; the husband can take her back, and the wife's mere request does not indicate consent to lose her inheritance. This is contrasted with the case where she explicitly asks for a 'ba'in' (irrevocable) divorce—there she does not inherit because her specific request shows her intention to sever all marital ties. The passage also notes a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding one ba'in divorce: in the Hanafi school, if a wife asks for talaq (unspecified) and the husband gives one ba'in divorce, she still inherits because her request did not specify bain. However, if she says 'طلقني بائنة', she does not inherit.

Key Terms
طلقة رجعية (talqah raj'iyyah): A revocable divorce that does not end the marriage contract immediately.
بائنة (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately upon pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife says to her husband 'طلقني' (divorce me) without further qualification. The husband divorces her with three revocable divorces. He dies during her idda. According to the Hanafi school, does she inherit?
A) Yes, because revocable divorce does not remove the marriage and her request was not consent to lose inheritance.
B) No, because she asked for divorce and thus forfeited her right.
C) Yes, but only if the divorce was one revocable divorce, not three.
D) No, because three divorces invalidate inheritance.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a wife who asks for divorce without specifying 'bain' inherits if she receives a revocable divorce, because the marriage is not fully dissolved and her request does not imply consent to lose her rights.

Sick Husband's Divorce at Wife's Request: The Lesser Principle
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wife's entitlement when sick husband acknowledges debt/bequest after divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the wife's entitlement when a sick husband acknowledges a debt or bequest after divorce, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's ruling from that of the Sahibayn and Zufar.

Core Idea
When a terminally ill husband acknowledges a debt to his divorced wife, she receives only the lesser amount to prevent collusion.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about financial arrangements made by a sick husband after divorcing his wife. The core concern is suspicion (tuhmah) of collusion to give the wife more than her inheritance share. Abu Hanifa takes a cautious approach, while his students differ based on the presence or absence of idda. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi jurists balance textual rules with the spirit of preventing fraud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two distinct contexts: (1) divorce at wife's request in the husband's terminal illness, and (2) post-hoc agreement (tasaduqa) that the divorce actually occurred in health and the idda passed.
The ruling depends on which scenario applies.
2
Locate the positions of the Imams
Abu Hanifa: lesser of acknowledged debt or bequest in both scenarios. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): full amount in tasaduqa, lesser in wife's request. Zufar: full amount in both scenarios.
The majority (including Zufar) agreed with Sahibayn on tasaduqa, but Abu Hanifa's view on wife's request was upheld by later authorities.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the ikhtilaf
The key factor is the presence of idda. In wife's request during illness, idda remains, creating suspicion of collusion. In tasaduqa, idda has elapsed, making her a stranger, so suspicion is removed. Abu Hanifa, however, argues that collusion is possible even without idda.
The Sahibayn use the absence of idda in tasaduqa to justify full entitlement.
4
Apply the principle to determine the wife's entitlement
Given a scenario, determine the amount she receives: lesser of the two amounts under Abu Hanifa; full under Sahibayn in tasaduqa; full under Zufar in both.
The practical difference is the amount of money the wife gets.
5
Distinguish related rulings
Note that in tasaduqa, testimony and zakat are valid because there is no suspicion, unlike in the wife's request scenario.
This shows that the rule is specific to acknowledgments and bequests to divorced wives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أبانها بأمرها في مرضه، أو تصادقا على أنه أبانها في الصحة ومضي عدتها، ثم أقر لها بدين، أو أوصى لها بوصية فلها الأقل مما أقر به أو أوصى لها إرثها عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الأصل أن قول الزوج المتهم مردود، وأن الوصية للوارث باطلة فيحتمل أن الزوجين إنما تواضعا على ذلك ليصح إقراره ووصيته لها، ويحتمل أن قد كان طلقها في الصحة حقيقة وانقضت عدتها، أو أبانها في المرض؛ لأن غرضه الإقرار أو الوصية فيثبت الأقل، ولا تثبت الزيادة على الأقل نفياً للتهمة، وهذا في صورة التصادق عنده وقالا: يجوز إقراره ووصيته. أما في صورة سؤالها الطلاق، فلها الأقل في قولهم جميعاً خلافاً لزفر، فإن عنده لها ما أقر أو أوصي؛ لأن الميراث لا يبطل بسؤالها وزال المانع من صحة الإقرار والوصية، ولهما في التصادق أنهما لما تصادقا على الطلاق وانقضاء العدة صارت أجنبية عنه، فانعدمت التهمة. ألا ترى أنه تقبل شهادته لها، ويجوز دفع الزكاة إليها، بخلاف صورة سؤالها الطلاق؛ لأن العدة باقية وهي سبب التهمة، ولا عدة في صورة التصادق فافترقا ولأبي حنيفة: في المسألتين: أن التهمة قائمة للاحتمال الذي ذكرنا والمواضعة عادة في حق الزكاة والشهادة فلا تهمة فيهما، وقالت الثلاثة مثل قول الصاحبين.
And if he divorces her irrevocably by her command in his illness, or they agree that he divorced her in health and her idda passed, then he acknowledges a debt to her or bequeaths to her, she gets the lesser of what he acknowledged or bequeathed to her of her inheritance according to Abu Hanifa; because the principle is that the statement of the accused husband is rejected, and the bequest to an heir is invalid. So it is possible that the couple conspired to that effect to make his acknowledgment and bequest valid for her, and it is possible that he actually divorced her in health and her idda passed, or he divorced her in illness; because his purpose is the acknowledgment or bequest, so the lesser is established, and the excess over the lesser is not established to negate suspicion. This is in the case of their agreement according to him. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: his acknowledgment and bequest are valid. As for the case of her asking for divorce, she gets the lesser according to all of them, contrary to Zufar, for according to him she gets what he acknowledged or bequeathed; because inheritance is not invalidated by her asking, and the impediment to the validity of acknowledgment and bequest is removed. And for them (the two) in the case of agreement, because when they agree on the divorce and the completion of idda, she becomes a stranger to him, so suspicion is removed. Do you not see that his testimony for her is accepted, and it is permissible to give zakat to her, unlike the case of her asking for divorce, because the idda remains and it is the cause of suspicion, and there is no idda in the case of agreement, so they differ. And according to Abu Hanifa: in both issues, suspicion persists due to the possibility we mentioned, and collusion is usual; but regarding zakat and testimony there is no suspicion. And the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar?) said like the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a husband in his terminal illness divorces his wife irrevocably at her request, or they later agree (tasaduqa) that the divorce actually occurred in health and her idda has elapsed. Then the husband acknowledges a debt to her or makes a will in her favor. The question is: how much does she receive? According to Abu Hanifa, she gets only the lesser of the acknowledged amount or the bequest, because the husband is suspected of collusion to give her more than her inheritance share. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that his acknowledgment and bequest are fully valid in the case of tasaduqa, since they agree the marriage ended in health, making her a stranger—thus no suspicion. In the case of the wife asking for divorce in illness, even the Sahibayn agree she gets the lesser, but Zufar disagrees and says she gets the full amount. The text explains the reasoning: in tasaduqa, the idda is over, so she is a stranger; in her asking, idda remains, fueling suspicion. Abu Hanifa, however, maintains suspicion in both scenarios because collusion is possible. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa in many later texts, but the text notes that the 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) agreed with the Sahibayn on tasaduqa. This is a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms
تصادقا (tasaduqa): Both husband and wife agree on a fact, especially regarding the timing and nature of the divorce.
الأقل (al-aqall): The lesser amount between a debt acknowledged and a bequest made, given to the wife to avoid collusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband in his terminal illness divorces his wife at her request, then acknowledges a debt of $10,000 and also makes a will for $5,000. According to Abu Hanifa, how much does the wife receive?
A) $10,000 (the debt) because it is a prior claim.
B) $5,000 (the lesser of the two amounts) because of suspicion of collusion.
C) $15,000 (both) because both are valid.
D) $0 because the divorce invalidates all claims.

Key Takeaway
The lesser principle prevents collusion: in terminal illness, the wife receives only the smaller of acknowledged debt or bequest, safeguarding other heirs' rights.

The Farr Inheritance Rule: Divorce in Imminent Danger
Ruling on farr with definition of terminal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a wife inherits from her husband after an irrevocable divorce due to 'farr', and apply the Hanafi definition of terminal illness to determine inheritance rights.

Core Idea
A husband who divorces his wife while facing certain death cannot deprive her of inheritance.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rule of 'farr' – a husband who divorces his wife while in a situation of imminent death (e.g., single combat, execution) is treated as if the marriage continues for inheritance purposes. The law penalises his attempt to defraud her of her share. We will examine the conditions that trigger this rule, the definition of 'deadly illness' (marad al-firar), and the exceptions where safety is probable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
State the rule: if a husband faces certain death (like mubaraza or being led to execution) and gives an irrevocable divorce, his wife inherits if he dies in that condition. Note that the divorce must be ba'in (irrevocable).
2
Distinguish sure death from probable safety
The text clarifies that situations like being besieged or in a battle line do not trigger inheritance because safety is the norm. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the rule correctly.
The principle: only when survival is highly unlikely does the rule apply.
3
Define marad al-firar (terminal illness) with Hanafi nuances
The passage gives multiple definitions: inability to fulfil needs outside the home, being bedridden, etc. Trace the different opinions among Hanafi scholars (Muhammad ibn Salama, al-Hindawani) and identify the 'sahih' (correct) view.
This definition extends the farr concept beyond physical combat to illness.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mubaraza
There is a weak narration from Abu Hanifa that the divorce of a combatant is like that of a healthy person. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that she inherits. Recognise how tarjih works within the school.
The weight of evidence supports the majority view.
5
Apply the rule to the wife as farr
The rule also applies in reverse: if the wife is in a life-threatening situation and initiates separation (e.g., apostasy, option of puberty), the husband inherits from her. Also note the special case of pregnant women, where inheritance attaches only after six months.
This symmetry ensures fairness: both spouses can be 'farr'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن إرثها. ومن بارز رجلاً، أو قدم ليقتل بقود أو رجم فأبانها ورثت إن مات في ذلك الوجه أو قتل، ولو محصوراً أو في صف القتال لا ولو علق طلاقها بفعل أجنبي، على ذلك الوجه لأنه فار. والأصل فيه أن امرأة الفار ترث في العدة، لكن حكم الفرار إنما يثبت بتعلق حق المرأة بمال الزوج، وإنما يتعلق حقها في ماله بمرض يخاف منه الهلاك غالباً بأن يكون صاحب فراش، وهو الذي لا يقوم بحوائجه في البيت كما يعتاده الأصحاء وإن كان يقدر على القيام تكلفا، والذي يقضي حوائجه فيه وهو يشتكي لا يكون فاراً، وقيل: إذا كان يتخطّى ثلاث خطوات من غير أن يستعين بغيره فهو صحيح حكماً، وإلا فهو مريض، والصحيح أن من عجز عن قضاء حوائجه خارج البيت فهو مريض وإن أمكنه القيام بها في البيت؛ إذ ليس كل مريض يعجز عن القيام بها في البيت كالقيام للبول والغائط. واختلفوا في المسلول والمفلوج وأمثالهما قيل مادام يزداد ما به فهو مريض، وإلا فهو صحيح، وعن محمد بن سلمة: إن كان لا يرجى برؤه بالتداوي فهو مريض وإلا فهو صحيح وقال الهندواني: إن كان يزداد أبداً فهو مريض، وإن كان يزداد مرة ويقل أخرى فهو صحيح، وقد يثبت هذا المعنى أي توجه الهلاك في غير المريض فيكون فاراً إذا أبانها فيه كما في المبارزة، أو التقديم ليقتل في قصاص أو رجم، وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة أن طلاق المبارز كطلاق الصحيح. ولو محصوراً أي لو كان الزوج محبوسا في حصن ونحوه، أو في صف القتال فأبان زوجته ثم مات أو قتل على هذه الحالة لا ترث؛ لأن الذي في صف القتال أو محصور في الحصن الغالب منه السلامة؛ لأن الحصن لدفع بأس العدو، وكذا المنعة فلا يثبت به حكم الفرار، وكذلك راكب السفينة والنازل في المسبعة أو في المخيف من عدوه والمحبوس ليقتل في حد أو قصاص، والمرأة في جميع ذلك كالرجل حتى لو باشرت سبب الفراق من خيار البلوغ والعتق والتمكين من ابن الزوج والارتداد ونحو ذلك بعد ما حصل لها ما ذكرنا من المرض ونحوه يرثها الزوج؛ لكونها فارة، والحامل لا تكون فارة إلا إذا جاءها الطلاق خلافاً لمالك بعد ما تم لها ستة أشهر.
And from her inheritance. And whoever (the husband) fights in single combat, or is brought to be killed by qisas or stoning, and divorces her irrevocably, she inherits if he dies in that condition or is killed. But if he is besieged or in the battle line, she does not inherit, even if he is besieged or in the battle line, and even if he conditioned her divorce on the action of a third party, in that condition because he is a 'farr' (one fleeing from death). The basis of this is that the wife of a 'farr' inherits during the idda. However, the rule of 'farr' is only established when the wife's right to the husband's wealth is attached, and her right to his wealth is attached only by an illness from which destruction is generally feared, such that he is bedridden, who does not fulfill his home needs as healthy people are accustomed, even if he is able to do so with exertion. And one who fulfills his needs while complaining is not a 'farr'. It is said: if he can take three steps without the help of others, he is legally healthy; otherwise he is sick. The correct view is that whoever is unable to fulfill his needs outside the home is sick, even if he can do so inside the home, as not every sick person is unable to do so inside the home, such as getting up for urination and defecation. They differed regarding the consumptive and the paralytic and their likes: it is said that as long as his condition worsens, he is sick; otherwise he is healthy. According to Muhammad ibn Salama: if his cure is not hoped for through treatment, he is sick; otherwise he is healthy. Al-Hindawani said: if he constantly worsens, he is sick; if it increases sometimes and decreases at other times, he is healthy. And this meaning (the threat of destruction) may be established in someone who is not sick, such that he becomes a 'farr' when he divorces her in that situation, as in single combat, or being brought to be killed in qisas or stoning. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that the divorce of a combatant is like the divorce of a healthy person. And if he is besieged, i.e., confined in a fort or the like, or in the battle line, and he divorces his wife irrevocably, then dies or is killed in this state, she does not inherit because the one in the battle line or besieged in a fort is most likely safe; because the fort is for repelling the enemy's harm, and similarly the fortification, so the rule of 'farr' is not established. Similarly, a passenger on a ship, one who alights in a predatory animal's lair or in a place feared from his enemy, and one imprisoned to be killed in a hadd or qisas. And the woman in all of this is like the man: if she initiates the cause of separation, such as option of puberty, emancipation, allowing the husband's son (to approach), apostasy, and the like, after experiencing what we mentioned of illness and the like, the husband inherits from her because she is a 'farr'. And a pregnant woman is not considered a 'farr' unless the divorce comes to her after six months have completed, contrary to Malik.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle of 'farr' (literally: fleeing from death). When a husband divorces his wife irrevocably while he is in a situation of imminent and almost certain death—such as engaging in single combat (mubaraza), being led to execution for qisas or stoning—his wife inherits from him if he dies in that condition. The reason is that, although the divorce is irrevocable, the husband is trying to deprive her of inheritance by divorcing her while facing certain death, so the law treats him as if he is still married for inheritance purposes. However, if the husband is merely besieged in a fort or standing in a battle line (صف القتال), she does not inherit because safety is the norm in those situations. The passage also defines what constitutes a 'sickness' that gives the wife the right to inheritance: it must be a condition from which death is generally feared, such that the husband cannot attend to his personal needs outside the home (e.g., going to the market). There is debate among Hanafi scholars about chronic conditions like tuberculosis or paralysis. The text also mentions that a wife can be a 'farr' if she is the one who initiates separation while in a life-threatening condition, and then her husband inherits from her. There is a difference with Imam Malik regarding a pregnant woman: according to Hanafis, a pregnant woman is not considered a 'farr' unless the divorce occurs after six months of pregnancy (because before that, the pregnancy may not be apparent or certain). This lesson combines the ruling on farr with the definition of marad (sickness) for inheritance purposes.

Key Terms
فار (farr): One who divorces his wife while in a situation of imminent death to deprive her of inheritance.
مبارزة (mubaraza): Single combat between two warriors in battle.
مريض الفرار (marid al-firar): A sick person who is likely to die from his illness, such that the wife's inheritance right attaches.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the key criterion for an illness to qualify as marad al-firar (terminal illness) for inheritance purposes?
A) The illness must be medically incurable.
B) The patient must be bedridden and unable to fulfill his needs outside the home.
C) The patient must be unable to take three steps without help.
D) The illness must be diagnosed by three doctors.

Key Takeaway
The farr rule prevents a spouse from using an irrevocable divorce to deprive the other of inheritance when death is imminent, and its application depends on the factual determination of 'certain death' or 'terminal illness' as defined by the Hanafi school.

The Fleeing Husband (al-Fār): Definition and Inheritance Ruling
Defines the fleeing husband and when wife inherits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the fār (fleeing husband) and apply the conditions under which his wife inherits, including the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the dueler.

Core Idea
A husband who divorces while facing imminent death is a fār; his wife inherits despite the divorce.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq opens the chapter on the divorce of a sick person by defining a special category: the 'fār' — a husband who divorces his wife while facing death. The central ruling is that his wife inherits from him during her 'iddah, even though the divorce is otherwise valid. The text then elaborates on what constitutes 'imminent death,' extending beyond illness to dueling or execution, and notes a divergence within the Hanafi school regarding the dueler.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a husband divorcing his wife while facing imminent death (the fār). The ruling is that his wife inherits from him during her 'iddah if he dies in that state.
2
Locate the dalil
The general inheritance verses (Quran 4:12) apply, but the specific ruling for the fār is derived from the principle of preventing evasion of the wife's right.
The text does not quote the verse directly but relies on the established rule.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The wife's right to inheritance attaches at the time of divorce if the husband is in a state of imminent death. This prevents him from fraudulently depriving her.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: in one narration, Abu Hanifa considered the divorce of a dueler (mubāriz) as that of a healthy person, so the wife does not inherit.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position is the general view that the fār's wife inherits in all cases of imminent death, including dueling.
The text reports Abu Hanifa's narration but does not explicitly prefer; later authorities uphold the general rule.

(ع) ورثت إن مات إلخ والأصل فيه أن امرأة الفار ترث في العدة، لكن حكم الفرار إنما يثبت بتعلق حق المرأة بمال الزوج، وإنما يتعلق حقها في ماله بمرض يخاف منه الهلاك غالباً بأن يكون صاحب فراش، وهو الذي لا يقوم بحوائجه في البيت كما يعتاده الأصحاء وإن كان يقدر على القيام تكلفا، والذي يقضي حوائجه فيه وهو يشتكي لا يكون فاراً، وقيل: إذا كان يتخطّى ثلاث خطوات من غير أن يستعين بغيره فهو صحيح حكماً، وإلا فهو مريض، والصحيح أن من عجز عن قضاء حوائجه خارج البيت فهو مريض وإن أمكنه القيام بها في البيت؛ إذ ليس كل مريض يعجز عن القيام بها في البيت كالقيام للبول والغائط. واختلفوا في المسلول والمفلوج وأمثالهما قيل مادام يزداد ما به فهو مريض، وإلا فهو صحيح، وعن محمد بن سلمة: إن كان لا يرجى برؤه بالتداوي فهو مريض وإلا فهو صحيح وقال الهندواني: إن كان یزداد أبداً فهو مريض، وإن كان يزداد مرة ويقل أخرى فهو صحيح، وقد يثبت هذا المعنى أي توجه الهلاك في غير المريض فيكون فاراً إذا أبانها فيه كما في المبارزة، أو التقديم ليقتل في قصاص أو رجم، وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة أن طلاق المبارز كطلاق الصحيح رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٣/١] (مستخلص)
Book of Divorce, Chapter of the Sick Person's Divorce. And from her inheritance. And whoever duels a man, or is brought to be killed by qisas or stoning, and divorces her, she inherits if he dies in that condition or is killed. Even if besieged or in battle line, no. And if he suspends her divorce on the action of an outsider, he is a fār. The origin is that the wife of the fār inherits during the 'iddah, but the ruling of flight is only established by the attachment of the woman's right to the husband's wealth, and her right attaches to his wealth by an illness that is predominantly feared to cause death, such that he is bedridden. They differed regarding the consumptive and the paralyzed: as long as his condition worsens he is sick, otherwise healthy. Muhammad ibn Salama said: if recovery is not hoped for, he is sick; otherwise healthy. Al-Hinduwani said: if it continuously worsens, he is sick; if it sometimes worsens and sometimes improves, he is healthy. This meaning—the fear of death—can also be established in the non-sick, as in dueling or being brought for execution. And in a narration from Abu Hanifa, the divorce of the dueler is like the divorce of the healthy.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It opens the chapter on the sick person's divorce by stating the general principle: a husband who divorces his wife while facing imminent death is called a 'fār' (fleeing from his marital duties), and his wife inherits from him during her 'iddah if he dies in that condition. The text then clarifies what constitutes 'illness' that makes one a fār: predominantly being bedridden and unable to perform his needs outside the home. Various scholars give definitions for chronic diseases like tuberculosis and paralysis: the reliable view is that inability to go outside makes one sick. The ruling is then extended to non-ill situations where death is certain or highly probable, such as entering a duel (mubāraza) or being brought for execution in qisas or stoning. However, there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that the divorce of one who duels is like that of a healthy person, meaning the wife does not inherit. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الفار (al-fār): A husband who divorces his wife while facing imminent death (from illness or other cause) to deprive her of inheritance.
المبارزة (al-mubāraza): A duel or single combat between two warriors before a battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor that makes a husband a 'fār'?
A) He must be physically fleeing the battlefield.
B) He must be suffering from an incurable disease.
C) He must divorce his wife while in a state of imminent death.
D) He must divorce his wife in the presence of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The wife of a fār inherits during her 'iddah when the husband divorces her while in a state of imminent death, a ruling that extends beyond illness to any certain or highly probable death situation.

Exceptions to the Fār Rule: Besieged Husband and Battle Line
Ruling on divorce when husband is besieged or in battle line

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a wife does not inherit from a husband who divorces while besieged or in the battle line, identify the underlying principle of predominant safety, and apply the same ruling to analogous situations and the reverse case.

Core Idea
If safety is predominant, the spouse is not a fār — inheritance is not cut off.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a person who divorces their spouse while in a life-threatening situation (the fār) does so to deprive them of inheritance, and the spouse inherits. But what if the danger is not dire? This lesson examines situations where the predominant assumption is safety, not death, so the inheritance rule changes. We will see that a besieged husband, a soldier in the battle line, and others in analogous circumstances do not trigger the fār ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenarios and ruling
The text first presents two specific cases: a husband besieged in a fortress (maḥṣūr) and a husband in the battle line (ṣaff al-qitāl). In both, if he divorces his wife and then dies or is killed, the wife does NOT inherit from him.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The reason given is 'الغالب منه السلامة' (safety is predominant). For the besieged, the fortress provides protection; for the battle line, the support of comrades makes death unlikely. This principle distinguishes these from the fār situation.
3
Recognize analogous situations
The text extends the same ruling to: a passenger on a sinking ship, a person in a dangerous place (like a jungle with beasts), one in fear of an enemy, and one imprisoned for execution in a hadd or qisas penalty. All are considered not fār because they are not actively fleeing from imminent death.
4
Note the reverse ruling for the wife
If the wife is in similar circumstances (e.g., severely ill) and she initiates the separation (through option of puberty, apostasy, etc.), then she is considered a fār, and the husband inherits from her.
5
Apply the special pregnancy rule and note the ikhtilaf
A pregnant woman is not a fār unless the divorce occurs after six months of pregnancy. This contrasts with the Maliki view, which considers her a fār from the beginning of pregnancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو محصوراً [ أي لو كان الزوج محبوسا في حصن ونحوه. (ع)] أي ولو كان الزوج محصوراً، أو في صف القتال فأبان زوجته ثم مات أو قتل على هذه الحالة لا ترث؛ لأن الذي في صف القتال أو محصور في الحصن الغالب منه السلامة؛ لأن الحصن لدفع بأس العدو، وكذا المنعة فلا يثبت به حكم الفرار، وكذلك راكب السفينة والنازل في المسبعة أو في المخيف من عدوه والمحبوس ليقتل في حد أو قصاص، والمرأة في جميع ذلك كالرجل حتى لو باشرت سبب الفراق من خيار البلوغ والعتق والتمكين من ابن الزوج والارتداد ونحو ذلك بعد ما حصل لها ما ذكرنا من المرض ونحوه يرثها الزوج؛ لكونها فارة، والحامل لا تكون فارة إلا إذا جاءها الطلاق خلافاً لمالك بعد ما تم لها ستة أشهر. [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٣/١] (مستخلص) أو في صف القتال : أي أو كان موازيا للعدو في الصف فطلق امرأته بائنا (ع) لا : لا ترث منه المرأة ولا يكون فارا؛ لأن الغالب فيه السلامة (ع) أجنبي: أي شخص أجنبي غير الزوجين بأن قال: أنت طالق إن فعل فلان كذا. (ع)
And if besieged [meaning the husband is imprisoned in a fortress or similar], and if the husband is besieged or in the battle line and divorces his wife then dies or is killed in this state, she does not inherit; because one in the battle line or besieged in a fortress, safety is predominant; for the fortress repels the enemy, and similarly the defending force, so the ruling of flight is not established. Likewise, the passenger of a ship, one descending into a dangerous place, one in fear of his enemy, one imprisoned to be killed in a hadd or qisas penalty, all are like that. And the woman in all these is like the man, such that if she initiates the cause of separation (option of puberty, emancipation, enabling the husband's son, apostasy, etc.) after she has obtained the aforementioned illness or similar, the husband inherits from her, for she is a fār. And the pregnant woman is not a fār unless the divorce comes to her, contrary to Malik, after she has completed six months.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that clarifies exceptions to the general rule of the fār. When the husband is besieged in a fortress or fighting in the battle line (ṣaff al-qitāl), the predominant assumption is safety because the fortress provides protection and the battle line has support. Therefore, if he divorces his wife while in such conditions and then dies or is killed, the wife does not inherit; he is not considered a fār. The same applies to a passenger on a ship that sinks, a person in a place of danger from beasts, or one imprisoned for execution in a hadd or qisas penalty—these are not situations of certain death. The text also discusses the reverse: if the wife finds herself in similar circumstances of illness or danger and she initiates the separation (e.g., by exercising the option of puberty, apostasy, etc.), then she is considered a fār and the husband inherits from her. A special rule applies to pregnant women: she is not considered a fār unless the divorce occurs after six months of pregnancy, differing from the Maliki school.

Key Terms
محصور (maḥṣūr): A person who is besieged or trapped in a fortress or similar place.
صف القتال (ṣaff al-qitāl): The battle line where soldiers face the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A person who divorces his wife while imprisoned for execution and then executed.

Key Takeaway
When the predominant assumption is safety, the spouse is not considered a fār, and the divorced partner does not inherit.
