Inheritance in Conditional Divorce by a Sick Husband: 16 Cases
Taxonomy and rulings for conditional divorce by a sick husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a divorced wife inherits from her sick husband by analyzing the type of condition, timing of ta'liq and shart, and identifying the mufta bihi position in cases of disagreement.

Core Idea
In conditional divorce during illness, inheritance depends on husband's intent to defraud; consent or unavoidability can override.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary lays out a comprehensive taxonomy of conditional divorces by a sick husband. The central question is: does the divorced wife still inherit? The answer turns on four categories of condition (time, third party action, husband's own action, wife's action) and the timing of the ta'liq and shart relative to the illness. Understanding these sixteen (or eighteen) cases sharpens your ability to apply the principle of 'farr' (evading inheritance) and to recognise legitimate ikhtilaf within and outside the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four condition types
The passage divides conditions into: arrival of a time, action of an outsider, husband's own action, and wife's action. Each is treated differently for inheritance.
2
Determine timing of ta'liq and shart
For each type, check whether the ta'liq (suspension) and the shart (condition) occurred during the husband's illness or health. This gives four subcases per type, totalling 16.
The wife's action type further splits into unavoidable/avoidable, making 18.
3
Apply the principle of farr
If the husband intended to deprive the wife of inheritance by making divorce conditional during illness, the wife inherits. The timing and his control over the condition indicate intent.
Husband's own action always triggers inheritance if condition occurs in illness.
4
Check exceptions: consent and unavoidability
If the condition is the wife's own avoidable action, she does not inherit even if in illness, because she consented. If unavoidable, she inherits.
Muhammad disagrees on unavoidable action if both in illness.
5
Note the intra- and inter-madhhab disputes
Zufar differs on case 3 (third party, ta'liq in health, condition in illness). Muhammad differs on wife's unavoidable action. The mufta bihi follows the two shaykhs.

The Book of Divorce. Chapter of the Sick Person's Divorce. 448. Chapter of the Sick Person's Divorce: Or by the arrival of a time, and the ta'liq and the condition are in his illness, or by his own action while both are in his illness, or the condition only, or if he suspended her divorce—the ta'liq and the condition—only, or by her action and she cannot do without it, and both are in illness or the condition, she inherits, and in other cases she does not. Or he suspended her divorce—the ta'liq and the condition—stranger? the mentioned forms. By the arrival of time: meaning he suspended her divorce by its arrival, such as saying, 'When the beginning of the month comes, you are divorced' (ع). And ta'liq: meaning while the ta'liq and the condition, that is the action of so-and-so or the arrival of the beginning of the month, are in his illness (ع). Or by his own action: whether it is something he cannot avoid naturally, like eating and drinking, or legally, like prayer and fasting, or something he can avoid, like speaking to Zayd or entering a house (ع). Or the condition only: meaning the condition is in his illness without the ta'liq (ع). And 'she cannot do without it' means she cannot do without that action, either naturally or legally as mentioned (ع). And 'both are in illness' means the ta'liq and the condition are in illness (ع). Or the condition: meaning the condition is in it only (ع). She inherits: the woman in these cases because he is a fugitive (farr) (ع). And in other cases she does not inherit (ط), meaning in other than the mentioned forms she does not inherit. That is when the ta'liq and condition are in health in all cases, or the ta'liq is in health when he suspended it by the action of a stranger or by the arrival of time, or however it is when he suspended it by her action which she cannot do without. The conclusion: the author's statement 'and if he suspended her divorce by the action of a stranger etc.' is of four aspects: either he suspends the divorce by the arrival of time, or by the action of a stranger, or by his own action, or by the woman's action, and each aspect has two subdivisions: either the ta'liq is in health and the condition in illness, or both are in illness. As for the first two aspects, i.e., when he suspends it by the arrival of time or by the action of a stranger: if ta'liq and condition are in illness, she does not inherit. As for the third aspect, i.e., when he suspends it by his own action: she inherits however it is if the condition occurs in illness, whether the ta'liq is in health or illness, and whether the action is something he can avoid or not, because he intended to invalidate her right by the ta'liq and condition or by the condition alone, since the condition resembles the cause, because existence is by it, so he becomes transgressive in a way, and his necessity does not nullify the right of others, like destroying the property of another in a state of necessity or sleep. As for the fourth aspect, i.e., when he suspends it by her action: if it is an action she can avoid, she does not inherit absolutely, whether the ta'liq and condition are in illness, or the ta'liq is in health and the condition in illness, because she consented to the condition, and consent to it is consent to the conditioned. And from this it becomes apparent that the issue has sixteen aspects, and each aspect has four divisions: either ta'liq and condition both in illness, or both in health, or ta'liq in health and condition in illness, or the opposite. Thus the total becomes sixteen aspects: First: ta'liq by action of a stranger, both in illness—she inherits. Second: ta'liq by it, both in health—she does not inherit. Third: ta'liq by it, ta'liq in health and condition in illness—she does not inherit, contrary to Zufar. Fourth: ta'liq by it, opposite—she does not inherit. Fifth: ta'liq by arrival of time, both in illness—she inherits. Sixth: ta'liq by it, both in health—she does not inherit. Seventh: ta'liq by it, ta'liq in health and condition in illness—she does not inherit. Eighth: ta'liq by it, opposite—she does not inherit. Ninth: ta'liq by his own action, both in illness—she inherits. Tenth: ta'liq by it, both in health—she does not inherit. Eleventh: ta'liq by it, — (page break). Page 449: The Book of Divorce. Chapter of the Sick Person's Divorce. And if he divorced her irrevocably in his illness, then recovered and died, or divorced her irrevocably and she apostatized, then embraced Islam and died, she does not inherit. And if the sick person with another illness, she submitted to the son of the husband, or the husband engaged in li'an = the woman, i.e., the woman after apostasy, i.e., the husband — and the ta'liq was in health and the condition in illness, she inherits. Twelfth: ta'liq by it, both in opposite—she does not inherit. Thirteenth: ta'liq by her action, both in illness—she inherits. Fourteenth: ta'liq by it, both in health—she does not inherit. Fifteenth: ta'liq by it, condition in illness and ta'liq in health—she inherits. Sixteenth: ta'liq by it, opposite—she does not inherit. And if we consider the division of her action into what she cannot do without and what she can do without, the division becomes eighteen aspects. In the first she inherits if both are in illness or the condition is in it only, contrary to Muhammad, and in the second she does not inherit at all, as we mentioned. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rulings on inheritance for a woman divorced by a sick husband when the divorce was conditional (mu'allaq). The author first identifies four categories of the condition: (1) arrival of a time, (2) action of a third party, (3) husband's own action, (4) wife's action. Each category is then subdivided based on whether the ta'liq (suspension) and the shart (condition) occurred during the husband's illness or health. The general principle is that if the husband intended to circumvent the wife's inheritance by making the divorce conditional and the condition occurs during his illness, she inherits, unless she consented to the condition (as in the case of her own avoidable action). The passage then enumerates sixteen specific cases, noting a disagreement with Zufar in case three: when ta'liq is by a third party's action, ta'liq in health, condition in illness—Zufar says she inherits, but the Hanafi position is she does not, because the husband's intention to defraud is not proven. A further disagreement with Muhammad is noted in the eighteen-case breakdown: when the wife's action is unavoidable, Muhammad says she does not inherit if both are in illness, but the relied-upon position (from the two shaykhs) is that she does inherit. The commentary explains the rationale: in cases where the husband acts, he is considered a 'farr' (one trying to evade inheritance duties), and necessity does not justify harming another's right.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon a future condition.
الشرط (al-shart): The future event upon which the divorce is conditioned.
فار (farr): One who tries to evade a legal obligation, here the husband trying to deprive the wife of inheritance by divorcing during illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case 11 (husband's own action, ta'liq in health, condition in illness), does the wife inherit according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the condition is the husband's own action and occurs in illness.
B) No, because the ta'liq was in health.
C) Yes, but only if the action is unavoidable.
D) No, because the wife consented.

Key Takeaway
The wife's inheritance in conditional divorce by a sick husband hinges on whether the husband intended to defraud, which is inferred from the type of condition and the timing of ta'liq relative to illness.

Inheritance After Recovery, Apostasy, Submission, or Li'an
Rulings on inheritance when specific events occur after sick divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of four post-sick-divorce scenarios (recovery, apostasy, submission, li'an) and explain the underlying legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Inheritance after a sick divorce hinges on whether the wife's action or the husband's recovery nullifies the cause of inheritance.

This lesson examines four specific cases that arise after a sick divorce: the husband recovers, the wife apostatizes, the wife submits to intercourse with the husband's son, or the couple engages in li'an. Each case has a distinct ruling based on the intent and effect of the events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four distinct scenarios presented in the passage: (1) husband recovers after sick divorce, (2) wife apostatizes and returns, (3) wife submits to intercourse with husband's son after bā'in divorce, (4) husband and wife engage in li'an.
Each scenario affects inheritance differently.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For each scenario, state whether the wife inherits: (1) No inheritance after recovery, (2) No inheritance after apostasy and return, (3) She inherits after submission if the divorce was irrevocable (bā'in), (4) She inherits after li'an initiated by the husband.
Note the exception in li'an: Muhammad differs if accusation was in health.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning for each
Understand the why: (1) Recovery annexes the divorce to health, so no intent to defraud. (2) Apostasy cuts off eligibility; returning does not revive marriage. (3) Submission does not cause separation; divorce already occurred. (4) Li'an stems from husband's accusation, making him a fugitive.
Compare with maintenance (nafaqa) which returns upon re-embracing Islam.
4
Identify internal Hanafi Ikhtilaf (li'an)
Note that Imam Muhammad differs from the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). He argues that if the husband accused in health, the wife's request for li'an makes her the cause of separation, so she does not inherit.
The two shaykhs respond that she was forced to request li'an to avoid disgrace, so she is not consenting.
5
Determine practical consequences
For each scenario, summarize the mufta bihi (operative) ruling for fatwa: The Hanafi school follows the views stated except where noted; the li'an ruling of the two shaykhs is preferred.
Be aware of Zufar's opposing view on recovery, which is not followed.

(مستخلص)
He died: meaning the husband after that while she is in the waiting period (idda). (ط, ع) She does not inherit [meaning the woman in both cases]. (ع) That is in both cases: as for the first, meaning when he divorced her thrice while sick, then recovered, then died in the idda, she does not inherit according to us. Zufar said: she inherits because he intended to flee (firar) since he executed the divorce in illness and he died while she is in idda. Our evidence is that since health occurred after the illness, it is annexed to health, so it becomes clear that the woman's right did not attach to his wealth, so the husband does not become a fugitive. As for the second, meaning when he divorced her thrice, then she apostatized—may Allah protect us—then embraced Islam, then he died from his illness while she is in idda, she does not inherit because her apostasy invalidated the eligibility for inheritance; the apostate does not inherit anyone, and after she embraced Islam, the cause of inheritance, namely marriage, did not exist. Unlike maintenance (nafaqa) which returns because its fall was due to loss of confinement, so when she embraces Islam she returns to his confinement and maintenance returns. (Mukhtasar, Ayni) She submitted to the husband's son: [in intercourse after he divorced her irrevocably in the idda, she inherits (ع)] meaning if he divorced her thrice or irrevocably (bā'in) in his illness, then the woman submitted to the son of the husband in intercourse, then the husband died while she is in idda, she inherits because the separation occurred by the irrevocable divorce, not by the submission. The submission did not remove her from eligibility for inheritance because foster relationship (muhrimiyya) does not contradict inheritance. Unlike if she submitted during the existence of the marriage, because then the separation is established by her submission from her side, so she becomes consenting to the nullification of the cause of inheritance, i.e., marriage, so she does not inherit. Similarly, if he divorced her revocably and then she submitted, she does not inherit. (Mukhtasar, Ayni) Or he engaged in li'an: [meaning the husband, by accusing his wife of adultery while healthy and engaging in li'an in illness, she also inherits. (ط, ع)] i.e., if he accused her of adultery in health and they engaged in li'an in illness, then he died while she is in idda, she inherits because the separation was due to his accusation, so he was a fugitive. There is no difference between the accusation being in illness or in health according to the two shaykhs. Muhammad said: if he accused her in health and engaged in li'an in illness, she does not inherit. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 265/1) Because this is like suspending divorce by her action which she can avoid, so she becomes the one nullifying her right by requesting the accusation, since li'an is only established by her request. Their evidence is that he forced her to request li'an because she has no alternative but litigation to repel disgrace from herself, so she does not become consenting to forfeiting her right. If the accusation was in illness, she inherits in all their statements. (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of inheritance after a sick divorce, focusing on four specific scenarios: (1) the husband recovers from his illness and then dies; (2) the wife apostatizes and then returns to Islam; (3) the wife voluntarily submits to intercourse with the husband's son; (4) the husband and wife engage in li'an (mutual curse). The author states that in the first scenario—where a sick husband divorces thrice, then recovers, then dies in the idda—the wife does not inherit according to the Hanafi school, contrary to Zufar. The Hanafi reasoning: once health is regained, the divorce is treated as having occurred in health, not in the original illness, so the husband is not considered a fugitive (farr). In the second scenario—apostasy and return to Islam—the wife does not inherit because apostasy cuts off inheritance eligibility entirely; when she returns to Islam, the marriage no longer exists as a basis for inheritance. Maintenance (nafaqa) is different because it depends on confinement (habs), which returns when she returns. In the third scenario—submission to intercourse with the husband's son after a bā'in (irrevocable) divorce—she does inherit because the submission did not cause the separation; the divorce did. However, if she submitted during a revocable divorce (raj'i) or during marriage, she would not inherit because her action would be a willing nullification of the marriage tie. In the fourth scenario—li'an initiated by the husband's accusation—if the accusation and li'an occur in illness, she inherits, as the husband caused the separation. The two shaykhs extend this even if the accusation was in health but li'an in illness; Muhammad differs, likening it to the wife's avoidable action (since she requests the li'an). The passage resolves this by saying the husband forced her to request it to avoid disgrace, so she is not consenting.

Key Terms
العدة (al-idda): The waiting period a woman observes after divorce or death before remarrying.
المطاوعة (al-mutāwa'a): Voluntary submission, here to intercourse with the husband's son after irrevocable divorce.
اللعان (al-li'ān): Mutual curse between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She inherits because the divorce was in illness.
B) She does not inherit because recovery annexes the divorce to health.
C) She inherits only if the husband was unconscious at the time of recovery.
D) She does not inherit because the wife apostatized.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance after a sick divorce is not automatic; specific events like recovery, apostasy, submission, or li'an can alter the ruling based on intent and legal cause.

Inheritance After Divorce in Illness: Recovery and Apostasy
Ruling on inheritance after divorce in illness with recovery or apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a husband divorces during illness then recovers, and explain why apostasy after divorce blocks inheritance but not maintenance.

Core Idea
Recovery annexes the divorce to health, so inheritance does not attach; apostasy destroys capacity to inherit.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two edge cases where a wife's inheritance after an irrevocable divorce might be expected but is denied by the Hanafi school. The first involves a husband who divorces while ill, then recovers before dying; the second involves a wife who apostatizes after divorce then returns to Islam before her husband's death. Understanding these rulings requires grasping the concept of evasion (firār) and the effect of apostasy on inheritance capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two separate cases: (1) divorce in illness followed by recovery, (2) divorce followed by apostasy and then reversion to Islam. Both concern the wife inheriting after the husband's death during her waiting period.
The common element: the husband dies while the wife is in her waiting period from an irrevocable divorce.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling for each
In both cases, the Hanafi school rules that the wife does not inherit. Confirm this from the text: 'lam tarith' appears for both.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the proof for the first case
The Hanafi argument: recovery after illness annexes the divorce to the time of health, so the divorce is not treated as an evasion. The wife's right to inheritance never attached to the husband's wealth. Compare with Zufar's opposing view that she inherits due to the husband's intent to evade.
Key concept: 'firār' (evasion) is negated by the occurrence of health.
4
Understand the second case: apostasy
Apostasy completely destroys the capacity to inherit (an apostate cannot inherit from anyone). Even after reversion to Islam during the waiting period, the marriage is already dissolved by the irrevocable divorce, so the cause of inheritance (marriage) does not exist at the time of death.
Distinguish from maintenance: maintenance returns upon reversion because it depends on confinement, which is reestablished.
5
Contrast inheritance with maintenance
Note that the passage explicitly contrasts inheritance with maintenance: maintenance returns upon reversion to Islam because its basis (confinement) is restored, but inheritance does not return because its basis (marriage) is not restored.
This illustrates that different legal bases lead to different rulings even in similar circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أبانها في مرضه فصح فمات، أو أبانها فارتدت فأسلمت فمات لم ترث. فمات: أي الزوج بعد ذلك وهي في العدة. لم ترث أي المرأة في الوجهين جميعاً. أما في الأول يعني فيما إذا طلقها ثلاثاً وهو مريض ثم صح ثم مات في العدة لا ترث عندنا، وقال زفر: ترث؛ لأنه قصد الفرار حيث أوقع في المرض وقد مات وهي في العدة ولنا أن المرض لما حصلت الصحة بعده التحق بالصحة، فتبين أن حق المرأة لم يتعلق بماله فلا يصير الزوج فاراً. وأما في الثاني وهو ما إذا طلقها ثلاثاً ثم ارتدت ثم أسلمت ثم مات من مرضه وهي في العدة لا ترث؛ لأن ردتها أبطلت أهلية الإرث؛ إذ المرتدة لا ترث أحداً، وبعد ما أسلمت لم يوجد سبب الإرث وهو النكاح، بخلاف النفقة حيث تعود؛ لأن سقوطها لفوات الاحتباس، فإذا أسلمت عادت إلى حبسه فتعود النفقة.
And if he gave her an irrevocable divorce during his illness, then he recovered and died, or he gave her an irrevocable divorce and she apostatized, then became Muslim, then he died, she does not inherit. 'So he died': i.e., the husband after that while she is in the waiting period. 'She does not inherit': i.e., the woman in both cases. As for the first, meaning when he divorced her thrice while he was ill, then recovered, then died during the waiting period, she does not inherit with us. Zufar said: She inherits, because he intended evasion by divorcing in illness, and he died while she was in the waiting period. Our evidence: When health occurred after illness, it is annexed to health, so it becomes clear that the woman's right was not attached to his wealth, so the husband is not an evader. As for the second, when he divorced her thrice, then she apostatized, then became Muslim, then he died from his illness while she was in the waiting period, she does not inherit because her apostasy invalidated the eligibility for inheritance; indeed the apostate does not inherit anyone, and after she became Muslim, the cause of inheritance (i.e., marriage) no longer exists, contrary to maintenance where it returns because its fall was due to lack of confinement; when she becomes Muslim, she returns to his confinement, so maintenance returns.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on inheritance after divorce during illness. It presents two scenarios: (1) The husband divorces his wife irrevocably while ill, then recovers, then dies while she is still in her waiting period. According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and his students), she does not inherit, contrary to Zufar who argues she should because the husband intended to evade her inheritance. The Hanafi proof is that recovery after illness annexes the divorce to health, so the wife's right to inheritance never attached. (2) The husband divorces her, then she apostatizes, then returns to Islam, then he dies from the original illness. She does not inherit because apostasy destroys the capacity to inherit (an apostate cannot inherit from anyone), and after reversion, the marriage is already dissolved, so no inheritance cause exists. The passage distinguishes maintenance, which does return upon reversion to Islam because it depends on confinement. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, with Zufar's view as a minority intra-Hanafi opinion.

Key Terms
أبانها (abānaha): He gave her an irrevocable divorce (bā'in divorce)
فار (fārr): One who evades the wife's inheritance right by divorcing in illness
مرتدة (murtadda): Apostate woman, one who renounces Islam

Key Takeaway
Recovery after a divorce in illness annexes the divorce to health, negating evasion; apostasy destroys inheritance capacity even if reversion occurs, but maintenance is restored.

Inheritance after Divorce in Illness: Wife's Submission and Lian
Rulings on inheritance when wife submits to husband's son or lian after accusation occurs in illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a divorced woman inherits from her husband when she has intercourse with his son during the waiting period, and distinguish between revocable and irrevocable divorce and between lian scenarios based on the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Inheritance after divorce during illness depends on the cause of separation: wife's submission after irrevocable divorce does not bar inheritance.

This lesson explores two edge cases of inheritance (irth) after divorce (talaq) during the husband's terminal illness. The first case involves the wife's submission to intercourse with her stepson; the second involves lian (mutual imprecation) after the husband's accusation of adultery. Both scenarios test whether the wife's actions nullify her right to inherit. We will follow the Hanafi reasoning and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and list the two distinct factual patterns: (1) irrevocable divorce in illness followed by wife's submission to stepson's intercourse; (2) husband's accusation of adultery (qadhf) in health, then lian in illness. For each, note the ruling on inheritance.
2
Analyze the first scenario: submission after irrevocable divorce
Understand why the wife inherits: the separation was caused by the husband's divorce (ibana), not her submission. Her act does not make her a consenting party to the end of marriage, so inheritance is preserved. Contrast this with submission during marriage or after revocable divorce (raj'i).
The key distinction is the type of divorce and the timing of the act.
3
Analyze the second scenario: lian after accusation
Examine the disagreement between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad. The two shaykhs hold she inherits because the husband compelled her to seek lian to clear her name; Muhammad holds she does not because she could have avoided the lian.
The dispute hinges on whether the wife's action in seeking lian was voluntary or compelled.
4
Trace the legal reasoning for each position
For the two shaykhs: the wife had no alternative but to litigate to defend her honor; thus she did not voluntarily nullify her inheritance. For Muhammad: her request for lian is an optional act she could have foregone, so she is the one who nullified her right.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text indicates that the relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs: she inherits even if the accusation was in health and lian in illness. This is the ruling issued for fatwa.
Later authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah) follow this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(مستخلص، عيني) أو لاعن: [ أي الزوج بأن قذف امرأته وهو صحيح ولاعن في المرض ترث أيضاً. (مستخلص)
She submitted to the husband's son: (i.e., in intercourse after he divorced her irrevocably during the waiting period, she inherits). Meaning if he divorced her thrice or irrevocably during his illness, then the woman submitted to intercourse with the husband's son, then the husband died while she was in the waiting period, she inherits, because the separation occurred by the divorce, not by the submission, and the submission did not remove her from eligibility for inheritance because the prohibited relationship does not negate inheritance, unlike if she submitted during the existence of the marriage; because the separation then would be established by her submission, and it is from her side, so she becomes content with the nullification of the cause of inheritance (i.e., marriage), so she does not inherit. Similarly, if he divorced her with a revocable divorce and then she submitted, she does not inherit. (Mustakhlas, Ayni) Or he performed lian: (i.e., the husband accused his wife while he was healthy and performed lian during illness, she also inherits). Meaning if he accused his wife in health, then they performed lian in illness, then he died while she was in the waiting period, she inherits because the separation was due to his accusation, so he is an evader. There is no difference between the accusation being in illness or health according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said: If he accused her in health and performed lian in illness, she does not inherit. (Mustakhlas) Because this is like a conditional divorce contingent on her act which she can avoid, so she is the one who nullified her right by seeking the accusation, since lian is only established by her request. But for the two shaykhs, he compelled her to seek lian; because she had no alternative but to litigate to remove the stigma from herself, so she is not thereby content to drop her right. And if the accusation was in illness, she inherits by all their statements. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct scenarios related to inheritance after divorce during illness. First: If a husband divorces his wife irrevocably while ill, and she submits to intercourse with his son (i.e., her stepson) during the waiting period, she still inherits from the husband if he dies in that idda. The reasoning: the separation (firqa) was due to the husband's divorce, not her submission. The submission does not affect inheritance eligibility because the resulting mahram relationship does not negate it. Contrast this with submission during marriage: then she would be consenting to destroy the marriage, so inheritance is lost. Also, if the divorce was revocable, submission does negate inheritance. Second: If the husband accuses his wife of adultery (qadhf) while healthy, then they perform lian (mutual imprecation) during his illness, and he dies during her idda, she inherits. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold this, even if the accusation was in health, because the husband compelled her to seek lian—she had to defend her honor—so she is not voluntarily giving up inheritance. Imam Muhammad disagrees: he sees her request for lian as an act she could avoid, so she is the one nullifying her right. All agree she inherits if the accusation itself occurred during illness. The relied-upon position is that of the shaykhs.

Key Terms
طاوعت (tāwa'at): She submitted willingly to intercourse
لاعن (lā'an): He performed lian (mutual imprecation) with his wife
قذف (qadhf): Accusation of adultery without witnesses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a husband accuses his wife while healthy and they perform lian while he is ill, and he dies during her waiting period, does she inherit?
A) No, because the accusation was in health
B) Yes, because the husband compelled her to seek lian
C) No, because she requested the lian willingly
D) Yes, only if the accusation was also in illness

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the wife's inheritance rights after divorce in illness are preserved unless she voluntarily and unnecessarily nullifies them, as in the case of revocable divorce or during marriage.

Inheritance in Ila': Health vs. Sickness
Ruling on inheritance when husband vows abstinence while sick or healthy and divorce occurs in illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when a husband makes ila' in health vs. sickness, and explain the legal reasoning and the response to the objection based on agency.

Core Idea
Ila' in health does not cause inheritance; only ila' in sickness does.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq distinguishes two scenarios of ila' (vow of abstinence) and their effect on a wife's inheritance. The ruling hinges on whether the husband was sick or healthy when he made the vow. The text also addresses a potential objection that draws an analogy to an agency scenario, clarifying why the wife does not inherit when ila' was in health.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two cases: (1) ila' while sick, (2) ila' while healthy.
The passage uses 'mariḍan' (sick) as a state.
2
State the ruling for each
For ila' in sickness, she inherits. For ila' in health, she does not inherit even if divorce occurs in illness.
The ruling is clear: no inheritance when ila' in health.
3
Understand the legal analogy
The author explains that ila' is like a conditional divorce (ta'liq) on the arrival of time. A condition made in health does not yield inheritance.
This is the core reasoning: the condition was set in health.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector argues that the husband could have revoked ila' by fay', so his failure shows evasion, similar to an agency case where the wife inherits. The response: revoking requires expiation (kaffara), making it not truly free; unlike dismissing an agent, which is without cost.
The key difference: ability is constrained by expense.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar argues she should inherit even if ila' in health, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) rejects this.
The text closes with 'khilāfan li-Zufar'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو آلى مريضاً، ورثت وإن آلى في صحته وبانت به في مرضه لا. مريضا: نصب على الحال من الضمير في آلي. وبانت به إلخ أي إن كان الإيلاء في الصحة فمضت أربعة أشهر وهو مريض، فبانت بمضي المدة في مرضه، ثم مات وهي في العدة لا ترث؛ لأن الإيلاء بمنزلة التعليق بمجيء الوقت والتعليق إذا كان في الصحة لا ترث. فإن قيل: في الإيلاء في الصحة ينبغي أن يكون فاراً؛ لأنه متمكن من إبطاله بالفيء، فإذا لم يفئ حتى بانت كان قاصدا لإبطال حقها فيرد عليه قصده فترث كما إذا وكل وكيلا في الصحة فطلقها الوكيل في مرض الموكل فإنها ترث؛ لأنه جعل مباشراً لتمكنه العزل، قلنا: لا يتمكن - وهو وجوب الكفارة من الفيء إلا بضرر فلم يكن متمكنا مطلقا بخلاف مسألة الوكيل؛ لأنه متمكن من عزله حتى لو لم يقدر على عزله حتى أبانها لم ترث. (مستخلص، فتح) عليه من في مرضه لا : أي لا ترث؛ لأن الإيلاء بمنزلة تعليق الطلاق بمضي الزمان فكأنه قال لها: إذا مضى أربعة أشهر فأنت بائن، ولا إرث بالتعليق في الصحة خلافاً لزفر.
Or if he made a vow of abstinence (ila') while sick, she inherits; but if he made ila' in his health and she became divorced by it during his illness, she does not inherit. 'While sick': accusative as a state of the pronoun in 'he made ila'. 'And she became divorced by it etc.': i.e., if the ila' was in health, then four months passed while he was sick, so she became divorced by the passage of the period in his illness, then he died while she was in the waiting period, she does not inherit because ila' is like a conditional divorce on the arrival of time, and a condition made in health does not yield inheritance. If it is said: In ila' in health, he should be considered an evader because he is able to revoke it by returning (fay'), so if he did not return until she became divorced, he intended to nullify her right, so his intent should be answered and she should inherit, just as if he appointed an agent in health and the agent divorced her in the illness of the principal, then she inherits, because he made him direct while able to dismiss him. We say: He is not fully able (for fay' requires expiation involving harm, so he is not absolutely able), unlike the agency case where he can dismiss the agent. Even if he could not dismiss until he divorced her, she does not inherit. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'In his illness does not': i.e., she does not inherit because ila' is like a conditional divorce on the passage of time, as if he said to her: When four months pass, you are irrevocably divorced, and there is no inheritance with a condition made in health, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage addresses ila' (a vow of abstinence from intercourse for four months) and its effect on inheritance when the husband is sick. Two scenarios: (1) The husband makes ila' while sick, the four months pass, she becomes divorced, and he dies during her idda — she inherits because the enactment was during illness, making him an evader. (2) The husband makes ila' while healthy, the four months pass while he is sick, she becomes divorced during his illness, and he dies — she does not inherit because the conditional divorce (ila' is akin to a conditional pronouncement) was set in health; health does not carry the presumption of evasion. The text then addresses a potential objection: Even if ila' is in health, the husband could have revoked it by returning to intercourse (fay'), so his failure to do so shows intent to evade, similar to appointing an agent in health who divorces during the principal's illness (where she inherits). The response is that the husband is not truly able to revoke without harm (he must perform expiation if he breaks the vow after the passage of time? Actually, fay' within the four months is without penalty, but after four months, he must either divorce or return? Wait, the text says 'he is not able' because returning requires expiation (kaffara) which involves harm (fidyah? Actually, if he breaks the oath after ila', he must pay kaffara yamin, which is a burden). So unlike an agent whom he can dismiss without harm, here returning from ila' has a cost, so he is not absolutely able. Therefore, the analogy fails, and the ruling stands. The passage ends by stating that this is contrary to Zufar, who would grant inheritance in the second scenario as well. The mufta bihi is the standard Hanafi position: no inheritance if ila' in health.

Key Terms
آلى (ālā): He made a vow of abstinence from intercourse for four months (ila')
فيء (fay'): Returning to marital intercourse within the four months to revoke the ila'
كفارة (kaffara): Expiation for breaking an oath, which is a burden that prevents the husband from being considered fully able to revoke ila' without cost

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on inheritance when a husband makes ila' (vow of abstinence) while healthy, and the divorce occurs during his illness?
A) She inherits, because the husband intended evasion by not revoking.
B) She does not inherit, because the ila' was made in health, and he is not fully able to revoke without expiation.
C) She inherits, because the divorce occurred in illness like the agency case.
D) She does not inherit, because ila' is not a valid divorce.

Key Takeaway
The wife does not inherit when ila' is made in health, even if the divorce occurs during the husband's illness, because the condition was set in health and the husband is not fully able to revoke without expiation.

Inheritance in Ila' from a Sick Husband
Ruling on three scenarios of ila' and inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the three scenarios of ila' and inheritance, and apply the qiyas to derive the ruling for a husband who makes ila' in sickness versus health.

Core Idea
Only ila' made in sickness grants inheritance — the time of the vow, not separation, determines the ruling.

Ila' is a vow to abstain from intercourse for at least four months. If the husband dies after the period passes, does the wife inherit? The answer depends entirely on when the ila' was made. This lesson unpacks the three scenarios laid out in the text and the qiyas that ties ila' to conditional divorce (ta'liq). Understanding this distinction prevents a common error of assuming inheritance always follows divorce in sickness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three possible combinations: (1) ila' in sickness, period passes in sickness, death during iddah; (2) ila' in health, period passes in sickness, death during iddah; (3) ila' in health, period passes in health, death during iddah.
The text addresses the second scenario explicitly; the first is deduced from the chapter context.
2
Understand the analogy to ta'liq
The Hanafi position equates ila' to a conditional divorce (ta'liq) upon the arrival of a time: if four months pass without return, divorce occurs. The ruling for inheritance from a sick person's ta'liq is already established.
This is a qiyas jaliy where the 'illa is the declaration in sickness that causes divorce upon a future event.
3
Apply the ta'liq rule to each scenario
For scenario 1: since the ila' (like ta'liq) was made in sickness, wife inherits. For scenarios 2 and 3: since the ila' was made in health, she does not inherit, regardless of when separation occurs.
The timing of separation is irrelevant; the critical moment is the vow.
4
Address the objection of fleeing (firar)
A potential objection argues that the husband could have nullified ila' by returning, so his failure shows intent to flee inheritance. The Hanafi response: nullification requires kaffarah and may cause harm, so he is not fully able compared to dismissing an agent.
This refutes Zufar's view that the wife inherits even when ila' was in health.
5
Note Zufar's minority view
Zufar holds that if ila' was in health but separation occurs in sickness, the wife inherits because the husband could have prevented the divorce. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position rejects this.
The text explicitly rejects Zufar's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو آلى مريضاً، ورثت وإن آلى في صحته وبانت به في مرضه لا. جواب المسائل الثلاث أي حال كونه صحيحا المرأة أي بمضي العدة باب طلاق المريض أو آلى مريضاً: [أي الزوج في المرض ومضت المدة وهو مريض، فبانت ثم مات وهي في العدة ترث أيضاً؛ لأنه فار. (ع)] أي إذا آلى من امرأته وهو مريض ومضت المدة، وهي أربعة أشهر فبانت، ثم مات الزوج وهي في العدة ورثت؛ لأن الإيلاء بمنزلة تعليق الطلاق بمضي مدته الخالية عن الوقاع، فيكون ملحقاً بالتعليق بمجيء الوقت؛ فكأنه قال لها: إذا مضى أربعة أشهر فأنت بائن، وقد تقدم أن التعليق إذا كان في المرض ورثت. (مستخلص، فتح مريضا : نصب على الحال من الضمير في آلي. (ع) وبانت به إلخ أي إن كان الإيلاء في الصحة فمضت أربعة أشهر وهو مريض، فبانت بمضي المدة في مرضه، ثم مات وهي في العدة لا ترث؛ لأن الإيلاء بمنزلة التعليق بمجيء الوقت والتعليق إذا كان في الصحة لا ترث. فإن قيل: في الإيلاء في الصحة ينبغي أن يكون فاراً؛ لأنه متمكن من إبطاله بالفيء، فإذا لم يفئ حتى بانت كان قاصداً لإبطال حقها فيرد عليه قصده فترث كما إذا وكل وكيلا في الصحة فطلقها الوكيل في مرض الموكل فإنها ترث؛ لأنه جعل مباشراً لتمكنه العزل، قلنا: لا يتمكن - وهو وجوب الكفارة من الفيء إلا بضرر فلم يكن متمكنا مطلقا بخلاف مسألة الوكيل؛ لأنه متمكن من عزله حتى لو لم يقدر على عزله حتى أبانها لم ترث. (مستخلص، فتح) من في مرضه لا : أي لا ترث؛ لأن الإيلاء بمنزلة تعليق الطلاق بمضي الزمان فكأنه قال لها: إذا مضى أربعة أشهر فأنت بائن، ولا إرث بالتعليق في الصحة خلافاً لزفر. (ع)
If he makes ila' while sick, she inherits. If he made ila' in health and she became separated (by that) in his sickness, she does not inherit. Answer of the three issues: any case while he is healthy, the woman [inherits?] with the passing of the iddah. Chapter on divorce of the sick: 'or makes ila' while sick' means the husband in illness and the period passes while he is sick, then she becomes separated, then he dies while she is in iddah, she also inherits because he is fleeing (from inheritance). That is, if he makes ila' from his wife while sick and the period passes, which is four months, then she becomes separated, then the husband dies while she is in iddah, she inherits because ila' is in the position of conditional divorce upon the passage of its period free from intercourse, so it is annexed to conditional divorce upon the arrival of time; as if he said to her: if four months pass then you are bain. And it has preceded that conditional divorce if in sickness she inherits. 'Mareedan' is accusative as state of the pronoun in 'aala'. 'And she became separated by it etc.' means if the ila' was in health and four months passed while he is sick, then she becomes separated by the passage of the period in his sickness, then he dies while she is in iddah, she does not inherit because ila' is in the position of conditional divorce upon the arrival of time, and conditional divorce if in health does not inherit. If it is said: in ila' in health it should be considered fleeing because he is able to nullify it by return, so if he does not return until she becomes separated, he intended to invalidate her right, so his intention is countered and she inherits, as when he appointed an agent in health and the agent divorced her in the illness of the principal, then she inherits because he made him direct agent due to ability to dismiss, we say: he is not able - which is the obligation of expiation for return except with harm, so he is not absolutely able unlike the case of the agent because he is able to dismiss him, so even if he was not able to dismiss him until he separated her, she does not inherit. 'Min' in 'in his sickness no' means she does not inherit because ila' is in the position of conditional divorce upon the passage of time, as if he said to her: if four months pass then you are bain, and there is no inheritance by conditional divorce in health contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Talaq al-Marid (Divorce of the Sick Person) states the ruling on inheritance of a wife who is divorced by ila' (vow of abstinence) from a sick husband. It covers three scenarios. First: if the husband makes ila' while sick, the iddah period passes, and the husband dies while the wife is still in iddah, she inherits. This is because ila' is analogous to a conditional divorce contingent on the passage of time, and such conditional divorce made in sickness results in inheritance. Second: if the husband made ila' while healthy, but the period passes while he is sick, and the wife becomes separated during his sickness, then he dies while she is in iddah, she does not inherit. The reasoning is that the ila' is still treated as a conditional divorce made in health, which does not grant inheritance. Third: if the ila' is made in health and the period passes in health, she does not inherit. A potential objection is raised: in the second scenario, the husband could have nullified the ila' by returning to his wife, so his failure to do so indicates intent to deprive her of inheritance, thus she should inherit. The Hanafi response explains that nullifying ila' requires a return that may involve harm (paying kaffarah), so he is not fully able to nullify without burden, unlike the case of appointing an agent who can be easily dismissed.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow by the husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for four months or more.
التعليق (al-ta'līq): Conditional divorce where the divorce is contingent upon a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a husband makes ila' while healthy, the four months pass while he is sick, he dies while his wife is in iddah, does she inherit according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because he was sick at the time of separation.
B) No, because the ila' was made in health, so it is like a conditional divorce in health.
C) Yes, because he could have returned but didn't, so he intended to flee.
D) No, because she is no longer his wife after the period passes.

Key Takeaway
A wife inherits from ila' only if the husband made the vow while sick, because it is analogous to a conditional divorce made in sickness.

Definition and Nature of Raj'ah: Continuation of Marriage vs. New Permission
Defining raj'ah and its conditions, methods, and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of raj'ah and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position by citing the Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Raj'ah in Hanafi fiqh is the continuation of existing marriage, not a new permission.

In this lesson, we explore the chapter on Raj'ah from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The text defines raj'ah as the continuation of marital ownership during the iddah period. A key point of contention is whether raj'ah merely permits intercourse (Shafi'i) or continues the marriage itself (Hanafi). We will unpack the evidence and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition
Read the opening line: 'استدامة الملك القائم في العدة' (continuation of existing ownership in the iddah). Recognize that this frames raj'ah as a continuation, not a new act.
The term 'istidāmat' implies preserving what exists.
2
Locate the Quranic proofs
Examine verses 2:228 ('وبعولتهن أحق بردهن') and 2:231 ('فأمسكوهن بمعروف'). Identify how the Hanafi school uses the word 'ba'l' (husband) to argue that marriage continues, and 'imsaak' (hold) to mean keeping the bond.
Also note the analogy with sale with option of rescission.
3
Understand the linguistic point
The term 'radd' (taking back) can mean continuation, as in returning an item that never left ownership in a sale with option. This supports the Hanafi view.
Shafi'i counters that 'radd' implies a new action after cessation.
4
Compare with al-Shafi'i's view
Al-Shafi'i holds that revocable divorce ends ownership, so raj'ah creates new permission for intercourse. Contrast this with the Hanafi view.
Practical consequence: intercourse during iddah is halal without explicit raj'ah in Hanafi, but requires it in Shafi'i.
5
Examine conditions and methods
Raj'ah is valid by explicit words, kinayah, or actions (e.g., intercourse). It is valid even without the wife's consent, with coercion or jest, and only within iddah after a revocable divorce (not after third divorce or bain).
Conditions: not accompanied by compensation, no bain description, not after three divorces (or two for slave woman).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجعة أي في بيان أحكامها استدامة الملك القائم في العدة. وتصح في العدّة إن لم يطلق ثلاثاً ولو لم ترض، الرجعية شرعاً الرجعة حال كونها براجعتك وراجعت امرأتي وبما يوجب حرمة المصاهرة. باب الرجعة هي لغة مصدر من رجع يرجع. (ع)] ذكرها بعد الطلاق؛ لأنها متأخرة عنه طبعاً فكذا وضعاً؛ لأنها شرعت لرفع سبب الحرمة، وهو الطلاق، والرفع أبداً يكون بعد الوقوع، والرجعة بكسر الراء وفتحها، والفتح أفصح، اسم من رجع رجوعاً، وإنما سميت بها؛ لرد أثر سبب الزوال. (مسكين، فتح) استدامة إلخ [ أي بقاء النكاح وطلب دوامه على ما كان (ع)] أي إبقاء النكاح على ما كان، وقوله: " في العدة" متعلق بالاستدامة؛ إذ المعنى تدارك دوام النكاح قبل زواله وقيد بالقائم؛ لأنه لا رجعة بعد زوال النكاح بالبينونة، والحاصل أن الرجعة عندنا استدامة النكاح الموجود قبل مضي العدة، وعند الشافعي: استباحة الوطء؛ لأن الطلاق الرجعي يحرم الوطء عنده؛ لأن ملك النكاح شرط جواز الوطء وقد زال بالطلاق. ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة : ۲۲۸)، والبعل هو الزوج والتسمية به حقيقة تستلزم قيام الزوجية، وقيامها يوجب حل الوطء، ولا دلالة في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ على أن ملكه قد زال؛ لأن الرد يستعمل للاستدامة كما إذا باع المبيع بشرط الخيار ثم فسخ وهو لم يخرج عن ملكه يقال: رد البائع المبيع فكذا هنا، وقال تعالى: ﴿فَأَمْسِكُوهُنَّ بِمَعْرُوفٍ ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۳۱)، والإمساك هو الإبقاء، فيكون أقوى دلالة على أن الرجعة استدامة، وهذه الآية الكريمة تدل على ثلاثة أشياء: الأول: مشروعية الرجعة، والثاني: قيام العدة؛ لأن بعد العدة لا يكون بعلاً، والثالث عدم شرعية رضاها. (مستخلص، فتح) في العدة أي ما دامت المرأة في العدة. (ع) إن لم يطلق ثلاثا ولم يقترن بعوض مالي، ولا صفة تنبئ عن البينونة ولا مشيئة و لم يكن بكناية يقع بها البائن وقد دخل بها وهي في العدة. (عيني، فتح) ولو لم ترض واصل بما قبله؛ لأن الأمر بالإمساك مطلق فيشمل حالة الرضاء وعدمه، وكذا تصح مع إكراه وهزل ولعب وخطأ. وتطليقتان في الأمة كالثلاث في الحرة لا يتصور فيها الرجعة؛ لأنها تحرم عليه حرمة غليظة. (عيني، فتح براجعتك متعلق بقوله: تصح أي تصح الرجعة بقول الزوج: راجعتك، إذا كانت حاضرة، و راجعت امرأتي في الحضرة ،والغيبة، أو ارتجعتك أو رددتك، وهذه كلها صريح، ومن الكنايات قوله: أنت عندي كما كنت أو أنت امرأتي، وهذه بالقول. (ط،ع) حرمة المصاهرة [أي وتصح الرجعة بالفعل مع الكراهة بما .إلخ. (ط)] أي تصح الرجعة بفعل يوجب حرمة المصاهرة كالوطء ودواعيه والمراجعة بالفعل مختلف فيها وبالقول متفق عليها والمراجعة بالفعل جائز عندنا
Chapter of Raj'ah meaning in explaining its rulings. It is the continuation of existing ownership in the iddah. And it is valid in the iddah if he has not divorced three times, even if she does not consent. Legally, raj'ah is while she is in iddah with your saying 'I take you back' or 'I take back my wife' and by what necessitates the prohibition of marriage. The chapter of raj'ah: It is verbally a masdar from raja'a yarji'u. It is mentioned after divorce because it follows it naturally and also in placement, since it was legislated to remove the cause of prohibition, which is divorce, and removal is always after occurrence. Raj'ah (with kasr or fatha of ra, fatha more eloquent) is a noun from raja'a ruju'an, and it is named so for reversing the effect of the cause of removal. 'Continuation etc.' means the remaining of marriage and seeking its permanence as it was. 'In the iddah' is related to continuation; meaning the catching up of the permanence of marriage before its cessation, and it is restricted to 'existing' because there is no raj'ah after cessation of marriage by bainuna. The summary is that raj'ah according to us is the continuation of the existing marriage before the expiry of iddah, and according to al-Shafi'i: permission for intercourse because revocable divorce prohibits intercourse according to him, because ownership of marriage is a condition for permissibility of intercourse and it has ceased by divorce. Our proof is His statement: 'And their husbands are more entitled to take them back' (2:228). 'Husband' (ba'l) means spouse, and calling him so in reality requires the existence of marriage, and its existence necessitates permissibility of intercourse. And there is no indication in His statement 'more entitled to take them back' that his ownership has ceased, because 'radd' (taking back) is used for continuation, just as when one sells an item with a condition of option and then rescinds while it has not left his ownership, it is said: the seller returned the item. So here too. And He said: 'So hold them with kindness' (2:231), and 'imsaak' is keeping, so it is a stronger indication that raj'ah is continuation. And this noble verse indicates three things: first, legislation of raj'ah; second, existence of iddah because after iddah he is not a husband; third, no need for her consent. 'In the iddah' i.e. as long as the woman is in iddah. 'If he has not divorced three times' and it is not accompanied by financial compensation, nor a description that indicates bainuna, nor a conditional clause, and it is not by a kinayah that results in bain divorce, and he has consummated with her, and she is in iddah. 'Even if she does not consent' is connected to what preceded because the command for 'imsaak' is absolute, so it includes the state of consent and its absence. Likewise, it is valid with coercion, jest, play, and mistake. Two divorces in a slave woman are like three in a free woman; raj'ah is not conceivable because she becomes prohibited with severe prohibition. 'I take you back' is related to His statement 'is valid', i.e. raj'ah is valid by the husband's saying: 'I take you back' if she is present, and 'I take back my wife' in her presence or absence, or 'I take you back' (irtaja'tuka) or 'I return you' (radadtuka), and all these are explicit. Among the kinayat are his saying: 'You are with me as you were' or 'You are my wife', and these are by word. 'What necessitates the prohibition of marriage' i.e. raj'ah is also valid by an action that necessitates the prohibition of marriage, like intercourse and its preliminaries, and taking back by action is disputed, while by word is agreed upon, and taking back by action is permissible in our view.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Raj'ah (revocation of divorce). It first defines raj'ah legally as the continuation of the existing marriage ownership within the iddah period. It is valid only during iddah, provided the husband has not given three divorces (or two in case of a slave woman), and even without the wife's consent. The text then explains the linguistic derivation and the reason for placing this chapter after divorce. A key part is the comparison with al-Shafi'i's view: the Hanafis hold that raj'ah is a continuation of the existing marriage, so marital rights including intercourse remain intact. Al-Shafi'i holds that revocable divorce severs ownership, and raj'ah merely permits intercourse anew. The Hanafi proof comes from Quran 2:228 and 2:231, which refer to the husband as 'ba'l' (spouse) and use 'imsaak' (holding) which indicates keeping the marriage, not creating a new one. The passage then details the conditions: raj'ah is valid through explicit words like 'raja'tuki' or 'raja'tu imra'ati', or by actions that make intercourse prohibited (e.g., intercourse itself). It is also valid with coercion or jest. Finally, it notes that with two divorces in a slave woman (equivalent to three in free), raj'ah is impossible.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The act of taking back a wife during her iddah after a revocable divorce, continuing the existing marriage.
استدامة الملك (istidāmat al-milk): Continuation of the ownership of marriage that already exists.
البينونة (al-baynūna): Final separation (bain divorce), after which raj'ah is not possible.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period after divorce before which the wife may remarry; raj'ah is only valid within iddah.
الإمساك (al-imsaak): Holding/keeping; used in Quran 2:231 to indicate retaining the marriage with kindness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of raj'ah?
A) A new marriage contract.
B) Continuation of the existing marriage.
C) Permission for intercourse only.
D) A form of conditional divorce.

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah in Hanafi fiqh is the continuation of the existing marriage, valid unilaterally by word or action during iddah after a revocable divorce.

Definition of Raj'ah and Its Validity in the 'Iddah
Defines raj'ah and states its validity condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of raj'ah as continuation of marriage, identify its validity condition (during 'iddah, not three divorces), and explain why no wife's consent is required.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is the continuation of marriage during 'iddah — not a new contract, and it does not require the wife's consent.

This lesson unpacks the opening of the chapter on Raj'ah from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It clarifies that raj'ah is not a fresh marriage but a continuation of the existing marital bond, and it remains valid only within the 'iddah period. Understanding this definition is key to distinguishing revocable (raj'i) from irrevocable (ba'in) divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic root
The author begins with the linguistic origin of raj'ah (return). Recognize that the term is derived from rujū'.
2
Grasp the legal (shar'i) definition
The legal definition is 'istidāmat al-milk al-qā'im fī al-'iddah' – continuation of the existing marital authority during the waiting period.
This implies the marriage was never fully severed.
3
Recognize the validity condition
The condition is that the 'iddah must still be ongoing and the divorce must not be the third (which makes it irrevocable).
If the 'iddah expires or three divorces have been given, raj'ah is invalid.
4
Note that wife's consent is not required
The text explicitly states 'wa law lam tarḍa' – even if she does not consent. This distinguishes it from a new marriage contract.
The husband's unilateral action is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجعة هي لغة مصدر من رجع يرجع. ذكرها بعد الطلاق؛ لأنها متأخرة عنه طبعاً فكذا وضعاً؛ لأنها شرعت لرفع سبب الحرمة، وهو الطلاق، والرفع أبداً يكون بعد الوقوع. والرجعة بكسر الراء وفتحها، والفتح أفصح، اسم من رجع رجوعاً، وإنما سميت بها؛ لرد أثر سبب الزوال. استدامة الملك القائم في العدة. وتصح في العدة إن لم يطلق ثلاثاً ولو لم ترض.
Chapter of Raj'ah. It is linguistically a verbal noun from raja'a yarji'u. It is mentioned after divorce because it is naturally subsequent to it, and thus arranged after it; because it was legislated to remove the cause of prohibition, which is divorce, and removal always occurs after occurrence. The word raj'ah with kasr of the ra' and fatha, and fatha is more eloquent, is a noun from raja'a ruju'an, and it is named thus for reversing the effect of the cause of cessation. It is the continuation of the existing marital authority during the 'iddah. And it is valid during the 'iddah if he has not divorced three times, even if she does not consent.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on Raj'ah (revocation of divorce). It begins with the linguistic and legal definition of raj'ah. Linguistically, it is derived from rujū' (return). The author notes that it is placed after the chapter on divorce because it is logically subsequent. The legal definition (Shar'an) is: 'the continuation of the existing marital authority (milk) during the 'iddah.' The ruling stated is that raj'ah is valid only if the husband has not pronounced three divorces (which would make the divorce irrevocable) and it takes effect even without the wife's consent. This is based on the Quranic verse: 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' (2:228), which implies that the marriage bond still exists. The condition of being during 'iddah is essential; after 'iddah, the divorce becomes bā'in (irrevocable) and raj'ah is not possible. The text emphasizes that raj'ah is not a new marriage but a continuation of the existing one.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj‘ah): Revocation of divorce by the husband during the 'iddah, continuing the existing marriage.
العدة (al-‘iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death, during which the wife cannot remarry.
استدامة الملك (istidāmat al-milk): Continuation of the marital authority, i.e., the marriage tie remains intact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is raj'ah valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only if the wife consents
B) Only if the husband has not divorced three times and she is in 'iddah
C) Anytime after divorce
D) Only if witnesses are present

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is the continuation of marriage during the 'iddah without wife's consent, provided the divorce is not the third.

Raj'ah as Istidāmah: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Establishes Hanafi view of raj'ah as continuation with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of raj'ah as istidāmah and explain the Quranic proof that distinguishes it from the Shafi'i view of istibāḥah.

Core Idea
Raj'ah continues the marriage, it does not recreate it — the husband remains husband during 'iddah.

The nature of raj'ah after a revocable divorce is a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Hanafis hold that the marriage contract persists; only the effects are suspended. This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq provides the Hanafi proof using two Quranic verses. Let us examine how the Hanafi school derives its position and how it refutes the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate that in the Hanafi school, raj'ah is the continuation (istidāmah) of the existing marriage contract, not a new beginning.
The wife remains a wife during the 'iddah period.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds raj'ah as istibāḥah — making intercourse permissible anew, because he believes revocable divorce ends marital ownership.
For Shafi'i, milk al-nikāh is required for intercourse; it ceases with divorce.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i rationale: since divorce removes the marital right, raj'ah must re-establish it.
This is based on the principle that ownership is a condition for permissibility.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi use of Quran 2:228 and 2:231. The term 'ba'l' (husband) implies ongoing marriage, and 'radd' (return) can mean continuation, as in sale with option. 'Imsāk' (retain) explicitly means preservation.
The verse 2:231 is stronger because 'imsāk' clearly indicates keeping.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, intercourse during 'iddah is permissible without a new contract; in the Shafi'i school, it is prohibited until raj'ah is formalized.
This affects a husband's daily life after issuing a revocable divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن الرجعة عندنا استدامة النكاح الموجود قبل مضي العدة، وعند الشافعي: استباحة الوطء؛ لأن الطلاق الرجعي يحرم الوطء عنده؛ لأن ملك النكاح شرط جواز الوطء وقد زال بالطلاق. ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة : ۲۲۸)، والبعل هو الزوج والتسمية به حقيقة تستلزم قيام الزوجية، وقيامها يوجب حل الوطء، ولا دلالة في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ على أن ملكه قد زال؛ لأن الرد يستعمل للاستدامة كما إذا باع المبيع بشرط الخيار ثم فسخ وهو لم يخرج عن ملكه يقال: رد البائع المبيع فكذا هنا، وقال تعالى: ﴿فَأَمْسِكُوهُنَّ بِمَعْرُوفٍ ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۳۱)، والإمساك هو الإبقاء، فيكون أقوى دلالة على أن الرجعة استدامة، وهذه الآية الكريمة تدل على ثلاثة أشياء: الأول: مشروعية الرجعة، والثاني: قيام العدة؛ لأن بعد العدة لا يكون بعلاً، والثالث عدم شرعية رضاها.
The summary is that raj'ah with us is the continuation of the existing marriage before the expiry of the 'iddah, while with al-Shafi'i it is making intercourse permissible; because revocable divorce prohibits intercourse according to him, since the ownership of marriage is a condition for the permissibility of intercourse and it has ceased with divorce. Our proof is the Almighty's statement: 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' (2:228), and al-ba'l means husband, and calling him that in reality necessitates the existence of marriage, and its existence makes intercourse permissible. There is no indication in the verse 'more right to take them back' that his ownership has ceased; because 'radd' is used for continuation, just as when someone sells an item with the option of withdrawal and then rescinds while it is still within his ownership, it is said: 'the seller returned the item' – so too here. And the Almighty said: 'So retain them with kindness' (2:231), and retention means preservation, so it is stronger evidence that raj'ah is continuation. This noble verse indicates three things: first, the legislation of raj'ah; second, the existence of 'iddah (because after 'iddah he is not a husband); third, the non-legislation of her consent.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents the central dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i regarding the nature of raj'ah. Hanafis define it as istidāmah (continuation) of the marriage contract, meaning the marital bond never ceased; only the effect of divorce was suspended. Shafi'is define it as istibāḥah (making permissible) of intercourse, arguing that revocable divorce removes marital ownership (milk al-nikāh), so raj'ah in effect re-establishes it. The Hanafi response relies on two Quranic verses: (1) 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' – the term 'husband' (ba'l) implies marriage is still intact; (2) 'So retain them with kindness' – the word imsāk means keeping, which indicates continuity. The Hanafi analysis of the first verse shows that the word radd (return) can mean continuation, not necessarily re-creation, as exemplified in sales with option. The second verse is stronger because imsāk explicitly means retention. The verse also shows that raj'ah is legislated, it requires 'iddah (since after 'iddah he is no longer a husband), and it does not require the wife's consent.

Key Terms
استدامة (istidāmah): Continuation; the idea that the marriage tie remains.
استباحة (istibāḥah): Making permissible; re-establishing the permissibility of intercourse.
البعل (al-ba'l): Husband; the term used in Quran 2:228 implies ongoing marital relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what does the word 'radd' (return) in the verse 'Wa buʿūlatuhunna aḥaqqu biraddihinna' indicate?
A) Re-creation of a new marriage
B) Continuation of the existing marriage
C) Perfection of divorce
D) Requirement of witnesses

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is a continuation of marriage, not a new permission; the husband remains husband during 'iddah.

Modes of Raj'ah: Verbal and by Action
Lists clear and kināyah words for raj'ah and rules on fi‘l.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify explicit (ṣarīḥ) and allusive (kināyah) words for raj'ah, and state the Hanafi ruling on raj'ah by action.

Core Idea
Raj'ah can be effected by words or by actions specific to marriage, but the sunnah is verbal with witnesses.

In this passage, al-Marghinani explains the two modes of performing raj'ah after a revocable divorce. He lists the explicit words that are universally accepted, the allusive expressions that require intention, and then addresses the controversial issue of raj'ah by action, where the Hanafi school takes a unique position. This lesson unpacks these modes and their legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Classify the words of raj'ah
Distinguish between ṣarīḥ (explicit) words that need no intention and kināyah (allusive) words that require intention. List the examples given in the text.
Explicit: 'rāja'tuk', 'irtaja'tuk', 'radadtuk'. Allusive: 'anti ‘indi kamā kunti'.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling on raj'ah by action
State that raj'ah by action is valid (sahīh) but disliked (makrūh). Note which actions qualify: intercourse and its foreplay.
The text says 'al-murāja‘atu bil-fi‘li jā’izun ‘indanā... lākinna makrūh'.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Recognize that Shafi'i holds raj'ah by action invalid when able to speak. Understand the basis: revocable divorce prohibits intercourse in Shafi'i, while in Hanafi it does not.
The ikhtilaf stems from whether the divorce prohibits intercourse or merely allows separation.
4
Determine valid vs invalid actions
Explain that only actions specific to marriage (e.g., intercourse, kissing with desire) constitute raj'ah. General acts like looking or touching without desire are not raj'ah.
The text excludes 'النظر واللمس بغير شهوة' because they are not exclusive to marriage.
5
Summarize the sunnah practice
Conclude that the preferred method is verbal raj'ah with witnesses and informing the wife, even if an action-based raj'ah has already occurred.
The text says 'فالأفضل أن يراجعها بالإشهاد ثانيا' – it is better to repeat with witnesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
براجعتك متعلق بقوله: تصح أي تصح الرجعة بقول الزوج: راجعتك، إذا كانت حاضرة، و راجعت امرأتي في الحضرة والغيبة، أو ارتجعتك أو رددتك، وهذه كلها صريح، ومن الكنايات قوله: أنت عندي كما كنت أو أنت امرأتي، وهذه بالقول. حرمة المصاهرة [أي وتصح الرجعة بالفعل مع الكراهة بما .إلخ. (ط)] أي تصح الرجعة بفعل يوجب حرمة المصاهرة كالوطء ودواعيه والمراجعة بالفعل مختلف فيها وبالقول متفق عليها والمراجعة بالفعل جائز عندنا وإن لم يقصد المراجعة لكنه مكروه، وعند الشافعي: لا تصح وإنما تصح بالقول عند القدرة عليه بأن لا يكون أخرس ومعتقل اللسان، وهذا بناء على أن الطلاق الرجعي يحرم الوطء عنده فيكون مثبتا للحل كما أصله، هو وعندنا: لا يحرم، فيكون استدامة، فكل فعل يدل على الاستدامة يكون به رجعة وهو فعل يختص بالنكاح بخلاف النظر واللمس بغير شهوة؛ لأنه قد يحل للطبيب والقابلة وتحمل أداء الشهادة في الزنا، ولا يكون بالنظر إلى شيء بدنها. سوى الفرج الداخل رجعة، وإذا راجعها بقبلة أو لمس فالأفضل أن يراجعها بالإشهاد ثانيا؛ لأن السنة من الرجعة بالقول والإشهاد وإعلامها
'Bi muraja'tik' is related to His saying: it is valid, i.e., raj'ah is valid by the husband's saying: 'I have taken you back,' if she is present, and 'I have taken back my wife' in presence and absence, or 'I have returned you' or 'I have sent you back,' and all these are clear (ṣarīḥ). Among the kināyāt (allusions) is his saying: 'You are with me as you were' or 'You are my wife,' and these are by word. Prohibited relationship by marriage [i.e., and raj'ah is valid by action with dislike (karāhah) by what causes the prohibition of affinity] i.e., raj'ah is valid by an action that necessitates the prohibition of affinity, like intercourse and its foreplay. Raj'ah by action is disputed, while by word it is agreed upon. Raj'ah by action is permissible with us even if he did not intend it, but it is disliked (makrūh). According to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid; it is only valid by word when one is able to speak, unless he is mute or tongue-tied. This is based on the fact that revocable divorce prohibits intercourse according to him, so it establishes the solution as its principle. According to us, it does not prohibit, so it is continuation. Thus every action indicating continuation is considered raj'ah, and it is an action specific to marriage, unlike looking or touching without desire; because that may be permissible for a doctor or midwife, or for bearing testimony in adultery. Looking at any part of her body except the internal private part is not raj'ah. If he takes her back with a kiss or touch, it is better that he perform raj'ah again with witnesses, because the sunnah of raj'ah is by word, with witnesses, and informing her.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage explains the modes of performing raj'ah. The words of raj'ah are divided into ṣarīḥ (explicit) and kināyah (allusive). Explicit words include 'rāja'tuk', 'irtaja'tuk', 'radadtuk', etc. Allusive words include 'anti ‘indi kamā kunti' (you are with me as you were). The passage then discusses raj'ah by action (fi‘l). According to the Hanafi school, raj'ah by action (such as intercourse or its foreplay) is valid even without verbal declaration, but it is makruh (disliked). This is because the act indicates continuation of marriage. Shafi'i holds that raj'ah by action is not valid; only verbal raj'ah is valid. The Hanafi reasoning is that revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse, so any action indicating marital intimacy can serve as raj'ah. However, actions not specific to marriage, like looking or touching without desire, do not constitute raj'ah. It is recommended (afḍal) to perform raj'ah verbally with witnesses and to inform the wife, as this is the sunnah.

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit words that clearly indicate raj'ah, such as 'rāja'tuk'.
كناية (kināyah): Allusive words that require intention to be effective, such as 'anti ‘indi kamā kunti'.
الرجعة بالفعل (al-raj‘ah bil-fi‘l): Raj'ah performed by action, such as intercourse or kissing with desire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions does NOT constitute raj'ah by action?
A) Intercourse
B) Kissing with desire
C) Looking without desire
D) Fondling with desire

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah can be effected by explicit or allusive words, or by actions specific to marriage, but the sunnah is to use words with witnesses.
